\section{KWS Post-processing}

    0:00 / 0:00Klicken Sie auf die Pfeiltaste nach oben, um das Geschwindigkeitsmenu aufzurufen. Regeln Sie dann mit den Hoch und Runter Pfeiltasten die Geschwindigkeit. Klicken Sie Enter, um die jeweilige Geschwindigkeit festzulegen.Geschwindigkeit1.0xKlicke auf diesen Knopf und diess Video stumm oder nicht stumm zu schalten, oder drücke die HOCH oder RUNTER Knöpfe um die Lautstärke zu erhöhen oder abzusenken.Maximal Lautstärke.Videotranskript
    Beginn des Transkriptes, zum Ende springen.
    VIJAY JANAPA REDDI: We are now going to talk
    about keyword spotting's post-processing stage, the last stage in the main loop.
    Again, as a quick reminder, we've got the initialization,
    and we've got the main loop.
    And the post-processing is something that comes about
    after you're done with the inference.
    And this is part of the main loop, which gets repeated over and over.
    Now, post-processing might seem like a little bit of a weird thing.
    You've already got your neural network that has made a prediction.
    What else do you really need to do?
    Well, often, when you're deploying things,
    there is a bit of post-processing that you have to do in order
    to get confidence in the results.
    This is something that is often not caught when you're just
    focused in the Colab where you're training a model,
    and you're not really seeing the full end-to-end picture.
    Let me give you a very concrete example.
    Let's say you're training your device to detect the keyword up.
    Here's a question for you.
    What happens if the user says upward?
    Is the machine supposed to wake up?
    Or is the machine supposed to ignore that?
    Well, obviously, your first reaction might
    be, well, the user said upward, not up.
    So yeah, the machine should not wake up.
    OK, well, that sounds fairly simple to you and me
    to be able to figure that out.
    But how does a machine know that?
    And the complexity really comes from the fact
    that it depends on where you read the sound.
    For example, if you look at this particular clip here,
    let's say the one second--
    if you've got a nice, clean one second of the full word
    that the user said and then you do your inference,
    well, is that going to be up or upward?
    Well, naturally, the network is not going
    to detect the word up here because it's nicely aligned for the full word.
    So it's going to say nothing.
    It's not a keyword at all.
    But then imagine if it so happened that, when
    you were creating your spectrograms in the preprocessing stage,
    you got your one-second word, the clip, right up
    until the mid-portion of the word, which means
    that you've only heard the word up, and you're taking a snapshot of that.
    And then you're feeding it into the network.
    What do you think is going to happen?
    Well, you're going to get a false positive there.
    The reason is because the machine only knows what it's just heard.
    It just saw the spectrogram from the word up in there.
    So it says yes, this is something that seems quite reasonable.
    This is totally possible.
    I'm not saying it happens all the time, but it's
    something that could totally happen and does in fact happen with our own model.
    So how can we improve this situation is the key question.
    Now, up is just one example.
    In reality there are many other examples like that.
    For example, think about no.
    Think about all the other words that are there, which happen
    to have no right at the beginning.
    The first one that comes to my mind is notable, "no-table."
    Well, if it picks up the word no and then you
    run it immediately through your inference,
    it's going to wake the machine up.
    So there are many other ones.
    In fact, I encourage you to kind of think of all the other words
    where such a weird situation might actually crop up.
    Now, how can we make things robust to this?
    Well, one important thing that we can do is that, after an inference is done,
    we don't immediately try and trigger the device
    to actually respond to something.
    Instead, we get the machine to listen to the spectrogram a couple of times.
    So we collect a couple of spectrograms in order to get confidence.
    In other words, we do preprocessing.
    We do inference.
    We collect some data.
    We don't immediately respond to it.
    We try and get some confidence.
    So we go back.
    We collect another bunch of data, do some preprocessing,
    get the new spectrogram, do the inference on that,
    see what the value ends up being, and repeat this process a few times.
    So the key takeaway is that, when we're doing keyword spotting,
    we actually do multiple inferences before we actually generate a result.
    Let me be a bit more specific here.
    So, for example, for every window, what we do
    is we store the results that come in from a new inference.
    And we calculate the new score for the keywords
    that the model is supposed to predict and keep a history of that.
    And we average them over multiple iterations.
    So, every time a new window comes in, we keep a running average
    of what the confidence is for a given keyword.
    If we do this enough times and we do this quickly enough,
    as in we make multiple inferences quickly enough,
    then we can actually have a very strong sort of a predictor.
    And that's the example I was showing here.
    So, for example, like let's say, at time step 1,
    you have some amount of confidence across these keywords like yes and no.
    Time step 2, after you do another inference,
    your confidence goes up that it's a yes.
    Time step 3, time step 4, time step 5, voila, in general, what you're seeing
    is that your confidence for that particular keyword is going up.
    So you're effectively averaging and building more hysteresis in there.
    And, in doing so, you can actually get a very good prediction.
    How do we do this in the code?
    Well, there is a function called ProcessLatestResults that actually
    implements this exact sort of behavior.
    So I'll go into the code later on when I do the screencast, but, for now,
    just understand that this is a core principle
    that you might want to leverage, as you build
    your own keyword spotting applications.
    You don't need to get into the details of this unless you really want to,
    but, by just calling this ProcessLatestResults,
    it's actually got the mechanisms to handle it.
    Now, once you've actually gotten some level of confidence,
    then you call the RespondToCommand.
    Now, the RespondToCommand's job is to do something
    based on a recognized command.
    In our case, it's all about triggering LEDs in different lights,
    depending on the keyword.
    For example, if it is a yes, it's going to be green.
    If it's a no, it's going to be red.
    If it's a word that it doesn't recognize, it's a blue.
    And that's how this example GIF that we're looking at here is working.
    The system is detecting a work.
    It's averaging things a couple of times by doing multiple inferences.
    And then it's actually seeing if it needs to respond.
    And that is how, my friend, keyword spotting really works.
    So post-processing is the key thing that I want
    you to walk away with in this video.
    It's not as simple as it seems.
    Now, not every application is going to have the same level of complexity.
    It really depends on what the use case is.
    For example, when we do the visual wake words or person
    detection model later on, you will realize that the post-processing
    is actually quite simple.
    In fact, you will find the keyword spotting is actually
    quite a complicated application by comparison to the other examples
    that we look at.
    And that's a good thing because I kind of
    want you to start with some of these core, important, fundamental building
    blocks rather than progressively increasing the complexity on you
    because then you would really not know where the ends would be.
    At least this way, you kind of know that keyword spotting
    is a rich enough application where you're
    getting exposed to some very important fundamental concepts
    about deploying an end-to-end TinyML application.
    In the next video, I'm going to try and summarize some of these core concepts
    for you so you know how to walk away in terms
    of understanding the code and the structure of these TinyML applications.
    And I'll see you there.
    Ende des Transkriptes, zum Anfang springen

    SPEAKER: In this screen cast, I'm going to walk you
    through the post-processing phase of keyword spotting.
    Now if you remember the post-processing phase
    comes right after the inference stage and it's got two major components.
    The first stage is the command recognizer
    and the second stage is the command responder.
    As a quick recap, the command recognizer job
    is to make sure it's detecting the right key word.
    In other words, let's say if the word is up,
    then it's got to make sure it has detected the word up.
    Not only that, it's actually got a more important job,
    which is to make sure it robustly detects the key word up.
    So for example, the user says upward, then
    its job is to make sure that the system does not accidentally
    trigger a false positive, as i had previously explained.
    So let's get into the code and try and understand how this is actually done.
    Now, the post-processing stage comes right after the inference stage.
    Line 162 is exactly where we had done the inference.
    After the inferences happened, you get an output.
    The output is effectively the output that
    has come out from the neural network, the Tiny comm network that we run.
    What happens immediately after that, is that we pass the output to process
    latest results.
    As you can see here, the process latest results function
    is taking output as an argument, it's taking the current time,
    it's taking a reference to found command.
    If it determines that a command has indeed
    been found, even after it's done it's robustness checks,
    then it will set this variable to be true.
    And for the command, it's going to assign it a certain score
    and will determine if a new command was found or if it was an old command,
    it would simply be false.
    With that said, let's dig right into process latest results,
    and see what is actually going on here.
    So I'm going to do a quick search for it and that brings us
    into the recognized command CPP file.
    And you can see that the output tenser that we
    were looking at in this particular function is called latest results.
    The first thing that's happening here, is
    that we're doing a quick error check on latest results Tensor.
    We want to make sure that the output tensor that
    is coming in from the neural network, is exactly of the form
    that we expect it to be.
    In other words it got dimensions equal to 2
    and we want to make sure that it's only got
    a single element in the first entry.
    And in the second entry, it's got to have kcategory count.
    In other words, at this point you've got to make sure
    that the output that's coming out in data one,
    the number of softmax probabilities you have there,
    matches the exact number of categories we are trying to label.
    Let me be more specific and say what is kcategory count.
    Category count is talking about the yes, no, silence and unknown.
    That's four different categories.
    So the count is actually four.
    And each one of these here is actually going
    to be telling us if it's yes, no, silence or no.
    And so it's making sure that, that output Tensor has a right format
    and that it's off the right data type.
    After that, because we will actually be collecting
    a queue of different inference records in order
    to get robustness of confidence as I had previously explained,
    we are actually making sure here is doing yet
    another round of self checking.
    That any new entry that is coming in, is actually being put in the right order
    into the queue.
    So, if that's not the case, we say the results
    must be fed in increasing time order.
    This is a simple sanity check, to make sure that all the results are new
    and no old results are getting pushed in.
    Because of the order of the results is not being maintained
    and that can lead to confusion in how we're processing the data.
    Assuming that everything is clear, the very first thing we do
    is we store the new result that is just come in, by pushing it
    into the back of the queue.
    The next thing we want to do, is to make sure
    that we're not keeping an infinite number of prior guesses.
    What we're instead doing, is that we're only
    trying to keep a limited number of entries in there.
    And the way we do that, is by trying to restrict ourselves to about 1,000
    milliseconds or so, which is what this variable has been defined to do.
    So there's a certain time limit, and if the old values
    kind of fall outside of this time limit, then they're
    effectively edged out by popping them out of the queue.
    Pretty simple.
    So long story short, the goal of this piece of code
    is to make sure that we're only looking at some reasonable number of items,
    most recent items.
    After that, we want to check if we have sufficient results.
    If we don't have enough of a history in there,
    then there's no point of actually making confident result.
    This is part of the robustness that this particular function
    is offering, in order to make sure that the prediction that's going to make
    is robust against different sorts of input variables.
    The next thing we go into, assuming everything is good,
    is we want to calculate what's the average score for each of the entries
    that I previously talked about and compute a running average.
    This is effectively a way of building confidence
    by making sure you're done the inferences a few times and then
    over that time, even within a one second period,
    you've got some confidence, that yes, I am actually looking at the right word
    based on what the user has seen.
    And that's the average calculation that's happening here.
    After we have done the average calculation,
    we're simply trying to find which category actually
    has the highest score in the list of variables that we have.
    And that's being done by simply looking at the current top index based
    on the average scores.
    And we're seeing if the current average score,
    is greater than the previous average score that was recorded.
    Once we find that, we are determining that the current top index corresponds
    to a certain category label.
    For example, here if I was to look up what the category labels, where
    you can see that the category labels in the default scenario
    are silence, unknown, yes, no and so forth.
    If I go right back to the file that I was in, which is a recognized command
    CPP, back to where we were.
    Now, we are at the next stage.
    The next stage what we're trying to do, is
    to make sure that there is some level of confidence in the command that
    is actually being analyzed.
    In other words, if this time we got a no, but just recently, we got a yes,
    and that sort of a bouncing is happening back and forth,
    then we don't have confidence in the results.
    And that's effectively what this code is trying to do.
    It's trying to see if the labels are actually coming out consistently.
    And if it turns out that everything is good,
    then we will say that yes, there is a new command that we have detected.
    At that point, we can happily go back to our primary function.
    I'm going to scroll down real quickly to where we we're.
    We were right here.
    We were inside this function.
    At this point, we have got some process data,
    we have actually analyzed the history of the commands
    and so we should be quite confident, in terms of what the command output was.
    So then we go on to the second stage.
    If you remember the second stage is the actual response stage.
    In the response stage, we want to trigger that LED.
    So let's go ahead and see what's happening inside the respond
    to command.
    The response to command is taking a couple
    of different input variables, which we will
    look at inside the respond to command.
    So, here we are, inside the Arduino command responder file.
    We are going to be working with LED's, as a means of actuating the system
    in response to a certain command.
    You might choose to actuate a motor or something to that effect, that's
    up to your liking.
    And you can see here, that the first thing this code actually does,
    is actually check if the code has been previously initialized.
    This is only done the first time when you're inside
    the main loop doing the steady state.
    This is not actually getting executed.
    The core part of this function is really this region.
    If there is a new command, and if it turns out that the command is actually
    a yes, then we want to set the LED that is green in color,
    to a low value, which will turn the LED on.
    Similarly, if the key word that was heard was no, then
    we want to set the red LED on.
    We do that by simply writing low.
    We are digital, right?
    Last but not least, if the word is unknown,
    then we set the blue LED to low.
    That causes the system to actually turn the blue LED on.
    Finally, the last thing to do is to see if there is a need to turn the LED off.
    For example, let's say the first time you say yes, now that turns the LED on.
    Well that's great.
    And then it stays on for a while.
    So what we're doing here, is that if the previous command happened
    to be like about three seconds ago and we turned the LED on, then
    here we want to simply turn it off.
    And that's what's going on here.
    And that's all there really is.
    At this point, you should be quite familiar with the overall structure.
    Preprocessing in summary, involves making sure
    that we're confident about the result that is being generated
    and that's being handled by process latest results.
    And after process latest results gives us a confidence response,
    then we determine what to do with that respond.

 \section{KWS Summary}
 
     0:00 / 0:00Klicken Sie auf die Pfeiltaste nach oben, um das Geschwindigkeitsmenu aufzurufen. Regeln Sie dann mit den Hoch und Runter Pfeiltasten die Geschwindigkeit. Klicken Sie Enter, um die jeweilige Geschwindigkeit festzulegen.Geschwindigkeit1.0xKlicke auf diesen Knopf und diess Video stumm oder nicht stumm zu schalten, oder drücke die HOCH oder RUNTER Knöpfe um die Lautstärke zu erhöhen oder abzusenken.Maximal Lautstärke.Videotranskript
     Beginn des Transkriptes, zum Ende springen.
     VIJAY JANAPA REDDI: You're almost to the end of understanding
     the keyword spotting application.
     After this, you're going to be working with Brian
     and actually deploying the keyword spotting model
     onto your embedded microcontroller.
     But, before I let you go over to Brian, I
     want to help you understand how to digest the vast amount of complexity
     that you might be feeling at this point.
     I know that we stepped through many different functions.
     Then we discussed about all the different topics
     that are involved around the code constructs.
     And we looked at a lot of code in the form of raw files.
     So, if you're feeling overwhelmed, don't worry.
     There's a method to the madness, and I'm going to teach you
     how to think about this systematically.
     First and foremost, there's a whole notion
     around setting up the environment.
     So there's a set of hows that are involved there.
     Then you go into setting up the ops and the interpreter.
     And that requires you to look at a couple of functions,
     usually in the setup portion of the code.
     Then we get into actually ingesting data from the microphone, which
     is the preprocessing part of the application.
     That can be a little bit complicated.
     And remember there, you have a device-specific portion,
     which is where you are dealing with the specifics
     of a particular microcontroller, for instance,
     and the way it interfaces with the I/O subsystem,
     specifically from the embedded sensor part.
     And there are non-device-specific aspects
     where you are just simply processing raw data,
     converting that input buffer data of audio signals into the spectrogram.
     Keep that in mind because those two things are a little different.
     And, once you have that, you invoke the interpreter with the right data.
     So you're typically copying that raw signal
     that you've perfected into features and then giving that over
     to the interpreter, which then performs the inference.
     And then it generates outputs.
     And that output is something that we act on
     by looking at what the actual output is from the network,
     be it a recognized word or not, and then decide
     if we're going to actuate some sort of LED or another subsystem.
     So most of the code is kind of largely sprinkled along these axes.
     So this could very well be a framework for the way
     you need to think about digesting this vast volume of code.
     That's lesson number one.
     Now, another thing around this is that there are many different components
     that we step through.
     Now, how do you kind of make sense of what to change and what not to change?
     Let's say you want to make some sort of tweak to the application.
     The way you've got to think about this is not thinking of the whole code
     because that's overwhelming.
     What you should really do is recognize the fact
     that the end-to-end application is really broken up
     into these four big building blocks.
     There's the setup.
     There's the preprocessing.
     There's the inference.
     And then there's the post-processing.
     And you tend to repeat the cycle between preprocessing and post-processing
     over and over and over.
     So pay attention to the different parts that I've
     listed out as examples that fall underneath each one
     of these components.
     For example, in preprocessing, you have the KISS FFT,
     or that's where you would do the MFCC.
     So let's say if you want to enhance the spectrogram that you're actually
     generating.
     Well, then you just need to focus on the preprocessing,
     and you don't need to really worry about the other pieces.
     But keep in mind though, if you change your preprocessing step here before you
     call the network, you've got to make sure your network has
     been trained accordingly in the earlier stages, like in course 2.
     Keep that in mind.
     You cannot simply change the preprocessing step and expect
     the network to perform better because the network has learned the features
     that preprocessing is actually doing.
     So it's extremely important to keep in mind
     that you would have to go back and retrain
     the network with a new set of preprocessing steps that you're doing,
     something that is actually quite subtle, but very important.
     Similarly, if you want to improve the network's performance
     through optimizations, well, then focus on what the network is.
     And, if you want to improve the confidence of the system,
     then focus on the post-processing side of things.
     You don't have to look at the whole code as one big, monolithic block
     sprinkled across different files.
     There is structure in the chaos that you might be witnessing.
     So, if you want to modify something, identify
     what you want to modify, and only focus on that part is the key lesson here.
     One of the reasons that I have specifically broken things down
     into the setup, preprocessing, inference, and post-processing
     is because it's a rather important message from the end-to-end application
     and design perspective that I want to convey over to you.
     Now, remember, right at the beginning of this course,
     I said that we're going to do things a little different here.
     We're not just interested in the model capabilities,
     and we're not just interested in coming up with the smallest neural network.
     We're actually interested in orchestrating the full flow
     and being able to design that end-to-end application
     so that it runs effectively.
     And, to that note, know that setup, preprocessing, and post-processing
     are just as important if not, in some sense,
     more important than the actual neural network model
     that we would actually do inference on.
     Why do I say this?
     Think about it from the execution latency perspective.
     You have to do setup once.
     And then, after that, you have to do the preprocessing, inference,
     and post-processing all in real time while the device is actually running,
     trying to detect your keyword.
     What that means is that they all add latencies.
     Now, this is just a pictorial representation
     of what the latencies might be.
     I'm not necessarily saying that preprocessing is always
     the most biggest chunk.
     It might be a smaller chunk.
     Post-processing might be bigger.
     Either way, the point that I'm trying to convey here
     is that they have different amounts of overhead.
     And those overheads can be sometimes more larger than the inference itself.
     So, when you're trying to optimize your device for performance,
     you need to keep this in mind.
     Similarly, from memory as well, you need to keep this in mind.
     Preprocessing, post-processing, setup, they all
     consume that limited amount of memory that we have on our microcontroller.
     So we need to keep that in mind, as we try and optimize the system.
     This is also the reason that earlier I talked to you about the embedded system
     software stack because that Mbed OS and all of those things
     actually eat up memory.
     So you need to be conscious about where you're
     going to tune the memory resource requirements if you have to do so.
     Bottom line is that, both in terms of execution latency,
     as well as memory usage, there might be different overheads that
     are coming from all the non-ML aspects.
     In other words, if I take inference out, there
     is some AI tax that is left behind, as I like to call it.
     And that AI tax is a pure overhead.
     And so, when we're designing applications
     to be efficient from an end-to-end perspective,
     as we need to do in keyword spotting to actually get it to work,
     well, then we need to also pay attention to how quickly we're
     able to do this processing.
     This is why, for instance, our version of the FFT in the preprocessing stage
     is actually very simple.
     It's actually not the best FFT that you can run.
     It's actually called KISS, which stands for Keep It Simple, Stupid.
     That's literally what it stands for.
     But you understand the notion of keeping things simple and why it's important.
     So keep AI tax in mind because that'll help you be a better TinyML
     engineer because you will understand what it really means
     to design an end-to-end application.
     Now, with all that said, I'm going to leave you
     in the good hands of Brian who's going to help you take that model
     and deploy it onto your embedded system.
     And also, later on, we will be changing that model in response
     to a new data set.
     So a lot of exciting stuff is coming up.
     So stay tuned for that, and have fun deploying the model.
     Ende des Transkriptes, zum Anfang springen
     Downloads und Transskripte

\section{Deploying the Pretrained KWS Model}

    In this reading, we are going to first generate a binary file representing the pretrained KWS model and then deploy that model to our Arduino using the Arduino IDE.
    
\subsection{Converting the TFLite Model File into a Binary Array}

    The first step in deploying the pre-trained KWS model is to take the quantized TensorFlow Lite model you created in Course 2 and convert it into a binary array that we can load onto our microcontroller. And don’t worry if you don’t still have the .tflite file from Course 2; we have provided a direct link to the staff copy in the following Colab.
    
    Please run this Colab and then, either leave the tab open with the printout of the final binary file and/or download the final .cc model file and open it up in a separate text editor. In either case, we will need to copy the data in that file into our Arduino sketch in the next section, so make sure you keep it in an easy to access place!
    
    https://colab.research.google.com/github/tinyMLx/colabs/blob/master/4-5-16-KWS-PretrainedModel.ipynb

\subsection{Updating the Arduino Code}

\begin{enumerate}
  \item Open the \FILE{micro\_speech.ino} sketch, which you can find via the File drop-down menu. Navigate, as follows: File → Examples → Harvard\_TinyMLx → micro\_speech.
    
  \item Navigate to the \FILE{micro\_features\_model.cpp} file and update the model. You can find that file by selecting it from tabs across the top of the Arduino IDE. If that file is not visible, you can navigate to it (or other additional files) by clicking on the downward facing triangle at the end of the tabs, which will open up a dropdown showing all of the files.
    
  \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-5.1-re.png}
    
    A Screenshot of the Arduino IDE showing that the main code starts on line 35 the type of the model and ends with a \{. When you are pasting in your model all of that definition needs to remain, You are only replacing the hexidecimal values which follow. Note all hexidecimal values are values in base 16 represented by 0-9a-f preceded by 0x so for example, 0x20, 0x00, 0x5f etc.
     
      \begin{itemize}
        \item Copy the binary model file contents from the \FILE{KWS\_yes\_no.cc} file into the \FILE{micro\_features\_model.cpp} file. Make sure to only copy the binary data inside the \{ \} as the variable type is different in the downloaded or printed \FILE{KWS\_yes\_no.cc} file (it is of type unsigned char in the \FILE{.cc} file, but it needs to be of type const unsigned char with the \PYTHON{DATA\_ALIGN\_ATTRIBUTE} in the \FILE{.cpp} file). If you lost your \FILE{KWS\_yes\_no.cc} file, don't worry, you can use the staff's copy!

        \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-5.2.png}

        A Screenshot of the Arduino IDE showing the end of the hexidecimal model values (as described in the previous image), ending with a \};. After that the next line says const int \PYTHON{g\_model\_len = 18712;}. Again you'll want to make sure to just update the numerical value.
    
        \item Next, scroll all the way down to the bottom of the file and replace the model length. Again, note that the .cpp file needs the variable to be of type const int while the .cc file will show unsigned int. Our suggestion again is to simply copy the numerical value.
        
        \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-5.3.png}
        
        A Screenshot of the Arduino IDE showing where to copy and paste the model length.
      \end{itemize}        
    
    \item Next save your changes. You will most likely see a popup as shown below asking you to save a copy of the example as all examples are treated as “read-only” by default. treated as “read-only” by default.
    
     \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-5.4.png}

      Screenshot of popup asking to save the sketch to a new location as it is ``read-only''.
    
      We suggest that you make a folder called e.g., TinyML inside of your Arduino folder. You can find your main Arduino folder either inside of your Documents folder or in your Home folder, and save it in that folder with a descriptive name like \PYTHON{micro\_speech\_edx\_pretrained}. That said, you can save it wherever you like with whatever name you want!

      \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-5.5.png}
    
      Screenshot showing a TinyML folder inside of the main Arduino folder.
\end{enumerate}


    
\subsection{Deploying the Pretrained Model}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item Use a USB cable to connect the Arduino Nano 33 BLE Sense to your machine. You should see the green LED power indicator come on when the board first receives power.
    
    \item As always, use the Tools drop-down menu to select appropriate Port and Board.
    
      \begin{itemize}
        \item Select the Arduino Nano 33 BLE as the board by going to Tools → Board: <Current Board Name> → Arduino Mbed OS Boards (nRF52840) → Arduino Nano 33 BLE.

        \item Then, select the USB Port associated with your board. This will appear differently on Windows, macOS, Linux, but will likely indicate ‘Arduino Nano 33 BLE” in parenthesis. You can select this by going to Tools → Port: <Current Port (Board on Port)> → <TBD Based on OS> (Arduino Nano 33 BLE). Where <TBD Based on OS> is most likely to come from the list below where <\#> indicates some integer number.

          \begin{itemize}
            \item Windows → COM<\#>
            \item macOS → /dev/cu.usbmodem<\#>
            \item Linux → ttyUSB<\#> or ttyACM<\#>
        \end{itemize}
      \end{itemize}

    \item     Use the rightward arrow next to the ‘upload’ / flash the code. You’ll know the upload is complete when you see red text in the console at the bottom of the IDE that shows 100\% upload of the code and a statement that says something like “Done in <\#.\#> seconds.”
    
    If you receive an error you will see an orange error bar appear and a red error message in the console (as shown below). Don’t worry -- there are many common reasons this may have occurred. To help you debug, please check out our FAQ appendix with answers to the most common errors!
    
    \item You should now have a working Keyword Spotting model for “Yes” and “No” deployed to your Arduino! To test it out, open the Serial Monitor. You should start to see the result of the model “Yes, No, Unknown, Silence” begin to display in the Serial Monitor.

      \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-5.6.png}
    
    Screenshot of the Serial Monitor showing the recognition of some utterances of yes and no as well as some unknown words.
    This should match the multicolor LED near the Bluetooth module on the board changing colors as follows (we’ve shown a picture of it turning green below):
    
    \bigskip
    
    Yes     = Green LED
    
    No = Red LED
    
    Unknown = Blue LED
    
    Silence = Nothing
    
      \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-5.7.png}

    Screenshot of the Serial Monitor showing the recognition of some utterances of yes and no as well as some unknown words. It follows the patter Heard X (Y) @Zms where X is what it heard (in our case yes, no, unkown) and Y is the score output by the model and Z is a timestamp of when this occured from when the Arduino was turned on.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Deploying a KWS Model with Your Favorite Keyword(s)}

    In this reading, we are going to first generate a binary file representing the KWS model with our favorite keywords from Course 2 and then deploy that model to our Arduino using the Arduino IDE (which will include some code changes).
    
\subsection{Converting the TFLite Model File into a Binary Array}

    Again we are going to first convert the .tflite file into a .cc file in Colab for use with the Arduino IDE. This time let’s use a model with different keywords (and a different number of keywords), so we can explore more of the changes you will need to make to deploy custom models. We’ve provided you with one option for a model in the Colab below. That said, we also invite you to use your favorite KWS model you created in Course 2 if you happen to still have the .tflite file! If you’d like to go back and train a new .tflite file, you can find the training Colab at this link.
    
    As with the pretrained model, we will be using the resulting .cc file, so make sure to download it or leave the tab open with the printout!
    
    \url{https://colab.research.google.com/github/tinyMLx/colabs/blob/master/4-5-18-KWS-FavoriteKeywords.ipynb}
    
    
\subsection{Deploying a KWS Model with Your Favorite Keyword}

    SPEAKER: All right.
    Let's now deploy a keyword spotting model with your favorite keywords.
    We'll start again with another basic micro speech example, just
    the default one that we opened up from the examples menu.
    And again, we will go and we will take our model file
    and we will copy just the binary contents of that file.
    And we will paste it into the file called micro features model.cpp.
    And we will just replace this giant binary model here
    with the binary model of the model with our favorite keywords.
    Again, be careful not to overwrite any of the syntax.
    And then this time, be extra careful to make sure
    that you copy over the model length, because it's
    very likely that the model length has changed since we no longer have
    just a yes/no model.
    We have something else.
    When we hit save-- again, it's going to ask us to find a new location
    to make a copy, since it's read only.
    And I'm going to call this one micro speech favorite.
    And I'll save it in that same folder that I
    saved the pre-trained model as well.
    So now we have this project and we've replaced the model.
    But we're not done yet because we've changed
    the number and the actual keywords themselves.
    So first we're going to go to micro features micro model settings.h.
    And in that file on line 40, right here.
    We're going to update the variable kCategory count
    and we're going to update that to be two plus the number of keywords we have.
    So that's silence unknown and then in our case, up, down, go.
    So that'll be five.
    We'll make sure to save that file.
    Then we will open micro features micro model settings.cpp.
    And here we'll see the actual list of the keywords
    where we'll need to change yes and no to up, down, and go.
    And we'll make sure to save that file too.
    OK.
    So now the last thing we need to do now that we have changed the model
    and changed the labels for that model, we're
    going to need to change the command responder.
    So we're going to open Arduino command responder.cpp.
    This is the file that actually does something when it finds a keyword.
    So in our case, we've been turning on LEDs.
    And you'll see right here in these if statements starting on line 54,
    it's looking to see if there's a variable
    found command, which is a string.
    So it's an array of characters, which are
    the letters of each of the key words.
    So found command 0 is the first letter.
    So it's looking for y for yes, n for no, and u for unknown.
    And that's how we're turning things green, red, and blue.
    So let's update this to our keywords.
    And the first problem you might immediately realize
    is, that up also starts with a u.
    But that's OK.
    What we can do is we can add a second part to this if statement.
    And we can say if the first letter was a u and if the second letter is a p,
    then we have a up.
    And so it's green for up.
    Down is simple.
    It's just an n.
    Red for down.
    And the go would also be easy, but I want to keep this unknown one.
    So I'm going to add actually, another if statement in here.
    And I'm going to say, if we find the d, up, down, go.
    If we find the g, then we're going to turn things blue.
    Blue for go.
    And then, what I'm going to do to unknown
    is I'm going to do again the double clause here
    to make sure that we are finding the correct word unknown and not just up.
    And what I'm going to do is I'm actually going to turn on all three LEDs,
    red, blue, and green.
    And together it will look kind of white.
    You'll be able to see the red and the green a little bit
    and the blue around the outside, but it will look mostly white.
    And so we'll call this white for unknown.
    And that's it.
    We save this file.
    So we've updated the Arduino command responder
    to respond correctly to our command.
    We've updated the micro model settings to the exact words
    that we actually are using.
    And one note here again, the order of these words matters.
    So if you trained it as up , down, go.
    It must be up, down, go.
    If you change the order, it will break things.
    So be careful when you train a model.
    Make sure you remember what order you put the key words in.
    We changed the command responder.
    This was the micro features micro model settings.cpp.
    We also changed the micro model settings.h.
    We changed the kCategory count and we changed the actual model
    itself, the micro features model.cpp.
    And once you've changed all of those, we are
    ready to first check and make sure that the board and the port
    are set correctly.
    And once they are, we're ready to compile and upload that to our device.
    And that's it.
    That's all you need to do and you'll be able to test out
    your model with whatever of your favorite keywords
    that you have deployed to it.
    So I hope you have fun with that.

\subsection{Updating the Arduino Code}

\begin{enumerate}
  \item Open the \FILE{micro\_speech.ino} sketch, which you can find via the File drop-down menu. Navigate, as follows: File → Examples → \FILE{Harvard\_TinyMLx} → \FILE{micro\_speech}.
    
  \item As before, navigate to the \FILE{micro\_features\_model.cpp} file and update the model. You can find that file by selecting it from tabs across the top of the Arduino IDE. If that file is not visible you can navigate to it (or other additional files) by clicking on the downward facing triangle at the end of the tabs which will open up a dropdown showing all of the files.
    
  \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-18.1-re.png}
    
  Screenshot of the IDE showing the downward arrow and the list of files that it opens up as described in the reading.
  
  \begin{itemize}
  \item  Copy the binary model file contents from the \FILE{KWS\_favorite.cc} file into the \FILE{micro\_features\_model.cpp} file. Make sure to only copy the binary data inside the \{ \} as the variable type is different in the downloaded or printed \FILE{model.cc} file (it is of type unsigned char in the .cc file, but it needs to be of type const unsigned char with the \PYTHON{DATA\_ALIGN\_ATTRIBUTE} in the \FILE{.cpp} file). If you lost your \FILE{KWS\_favorite.cc} file, don't worry, you can use the staff's copy!
   
  \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-18.1-re.png}
    
    A screenshot of the Arduino IDE show the arrow on the top right corner of the screen that has been clicked to show all of the additional files in this Arudino sketch. The file \FILE{micro\_features\_model.cpp} is highlighted as it needs to be selected to allow us to update the model.
    
    \item Next, scroll all the way down to the bottom of the file and replace the model length. Again, note that the .cpp file needs the variable to be of type const int while the .cc file will show unsigned int. Our suggestion again is to simply copy the numerical value.
    
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-18.3.png}
    
    A Screenshot of the Arduino IDE showing where to copy and paste the model length.
 
     \end{itemize}

  \item   Next, save your changes. You will most likely see a popup as shown below asking you to save a copy of the example as all examples are treated as “read-only” by default.
  
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-18.4.png}

  Screenshot of popup asking to save the sketch to a new location as it is “read-only”.
  
  Again, we suggest that you make a folder called e.g., TinyML inside of your Arduino folder. You can find your main Arduino folder either inside of your Documents folder or in your Home folder, and save it in that folder with a descriptive name like \FILE{micro\_speech\_favorite}. That said, you can save it wherever you like with whatever name you want!

    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-18.5.png}

    Screenshot showing a TinyML folder inside of the main Arduino folder.
    
  \item    The two other things we need to change are the count and list of keywords in the model settings files and the output response file. As we do that in the rest of this document, we are going to assume that you used our model with the Keywords “up,down,go.” If you choose to use different keywords, make sure that you update the following steps accordingly.
   
  \item  Navigate to the \FILE{micro\_features\_micro\_model\_settings.h} file and scroll down to line 40 and change the value of the \PYTHON{kCategoryCount} variable to be two more than the number of keywords you selected (one extra for silence and one extra for unknown). For example, if you had 3 keywords (as we do in our example model), we need to update the variable to be $3 + 2 = 5$ as shown:
  
  \PYTHON{constexpr int}
  
  \PYTHON{kCategoryCount = 5;}
    
  \item  Then, navigate to the \FILE{micro\_features\_micro\_model\_settings.cpp} file. You’ll find that it includes the .h file and otherwise has a single array. Update the values of that array to match your keywords. Make sure to leave ``silence'' and ``unknown'' in the list as well, and make sure to list your keywords in the order that you listed them on the training script. So, in the case of our example model, we would update the array to be:
  
  \begin{lstlisting}
    const char* kCategoryLabels[kCategoryCount] = {
        
        "silence",
        
        "unknown",
        
        "up",
        
        "down",
        
        "go",
        
    };
  \end{lstlisting}  
    
  \item  Finally, navigate to the \FILE{arduino\_command\_responder.cpp} file. Scroll down to line 54. There, you will see a series of if statements that control how the Arduino responds to each command. They are each structured to look at the value of the \PYTHON{found\_command} variable. This variable will be the string of the keyword (or ``silence'' or ``unknown'') you entered into the \PYTHON{kCategoryLabels} array in the previous step. So, by comparing the [0] value in that string, the if statements are simply looking at the first letter! In each if statement, you’ll also see a digitalWrite to either the LEDG, LEDR, or LEDB variable which is used to turn on the appropriate color LED. So, if we wanted to adapt this to our three keywords and, for example, only turn on the single colors for our keywords and turn all of the colors on for unknown, which will come out look white, we could update those three if statements to the following:
  
  \begin{lstlisting}
    // Red for up -- note here that you do not need to index
    
    //               into the first letter only, just a unique
    
    //               letter combination in the keyword! That
    
    //               said make sure you do not index beyond the
    
    //               end of ANY keyword or you will get an error!,
    
    if(found_command[1] == 'p') {
        
        last_command_time = current_time;
        
        digitalWrite(LEDR, LOW);
        
    }
  \end{lstlisting}  
    
  \begin{lstlisting}
    // Green for down
    
    if(found_command[0] == 'd') {
        
        last_command_time = current_time;
        
        digitalWrite(LEDG, LOW);
        
    }
  \end{lstlisting}  
    
  \begin{lstlisting}
    // Blue for go
    
    if(found_command[0] == 'g') {
        
        last_command_time = current_time;
        
        digitalWrite(LEDB, LOW);
        
    }
  \end{lstlisting}  
    
  \begin{lstlisting}
    // All three for unknown (white)
    
    if(found_command[1] == 'n') {
        
        last_command_time = current_time;
        
        digitalWrite(LEDR, LOW);
        
        digitalWrite(LEDG, LOW);
        
        digitalWrite(LEDB, LOW);
        
    }
  \end{lstlisting}  
  
\end{enumerate}

    
\subsection{Deploying the New Model}

\begin{enumerate}
  \item Use a USB cable to connect the Arduino Nano 33 BLE Sense to your machine. You should see the green LED power indicator come on when the board first receives power.
  
  \item  As always, use the Tools drop-down menu to select the appropriate Port and Board.
    
     \begin{itemize}
        
        \item Select the Arduino Nano 33 BLE as the board by going to Tools → Board: <Current Board Name> → Arduino Mbed OS Boards (nRF52840) → Arduino Nano 33 BLE.
   
        \item    Then, select the USB Port associated with your board. This will appear differently on Windows, macOS, Linux, but will likely indicate ‘Arduino Nano 33 BLE” in parenthesis. You can select this by going to Tools → Port: <Current Port (Board on Port)> → <TBD Based on OS> (Arduino Nano 33 BLE). Where <TBD Based on OS> is most likely to come from the list below where <\#> indicates some integer number.
    
      \begin{itemize}
        \item Windows → COM<\#>
        \item macOS → /dev/cu.usbmodem<\#>
        \item Linux → ttyUSB<\#> or ttyACM<\#>
       \end{itemize}
    \end{itemize}

    \item Use the rightward arrow next to the ‘upload’ / flash the code. You’ll know the upload is complete when you see red text in the console at the bottom of the IDE that shows 100\% upload of the code and a statement that says something like “Done in <\#.\#> seconds.”
    
    If you receive an error, you will see an orange error bar appear and a red error message in the console (as shown below). Don’t worry -- there are many common reasons this may have occurred. To help you debug, please check out our FAQ appendix with answers to the most common errors!
    
    \item You should now have a working Keyword Spotting model for either your favorite keywords from Course 2 or our model for “up,down,go” deployed to your Arduino! To test it out, open the Serial Monitor. You should start to see the result of the model begin to display in the Serial Monitor and the LED’s light up according to how you instructed them in the previous section!
   
\end{enumerate}
        
        
% 1.6: Custom Dataset Engineering for Keyword Spotting

\section{Recap: Dataset Engineering}

    In this module, you will learn about the nuances of data engineering, and develop your own dataset which you will use to train and deploy a custom keyword spotting model!
    
    Within these courses so far, we have used numerous datasets to train our algorithms. The existence of a dataset spared us from having to tackle the most time-consuming components of the machine learning pipeline: the collection and wrangling of data. In this section of the course, we are going to create our own dataset to perform keyword spotting, and expose you to some of the challenges that come along with the process, such as dataset bias, the need for edge cases, and the difficulty associated with procuring large quantities of data. This procedure is aptly named dataset engineering.
    
    Data engineering is an important aspect of supervised learning and entails the specification of the dataset, as well as the procedures for collecting and processing data. Naturally, this involves defining what features will be present, the structure of the data (e.g., images, time series, row tuples), and other characteristics.
    
    One of the key features of data engineering is identifying potential sources of data, which may come from external sources (free or purchased), crowdsourcing, existing users of a service, sensors, or other means. When developing our dataset, it is imperative that we consider our use case at all times, since this is our ultimate goal.
    
    A natural corollary of this is that we must determine the environment our device will be in once deployed. For example, if an algorithm processing sound data expects to be used on the street, it is necessary to make recordings in the presence of adversarial sounds such as construction work, loud vehicles, as well as other background noise. We may have to decide what time of day images are taken, and also make sure that each of the classes in our dataset has roughly equal representation to prevent biasing to the majority class. 
    
\section{Introduce Custom Dataset for KWS}

    but I want to do a recap on that portion because I
    want to help you understand the systemic issues that are involved with actually
    collecting a data set before you go on to actually building your own data set.
    Now recall that data sets require a significant amount of effort,
    both in terms of the labeling that's involved,
    as well as the manual effort that's actually involved in collecting

    across the planet.
    MS COCO, from Microsoft, has over 2.5 million
    labeled images specifically to deal with detection related use cases.
    ImageNet, which is used widely for image classification tasks,
    was created by Fei-Fei Li at Princeton with her students,
    and that has over millions of images at this point.
    Similarly, Waymo and KITTI, which are specifically
    tuned for actually deploying autonomous vehicles on the road,
    have had to have a massive amount of data set collected, all of which
    costs a lot of money, time, and effort.
    So the question is this.
    All these folks have gone through the experience
    of actually collecting the data set and know the nuances behind it.
    So the question here for us is, how do we build our own data set?
    What are the data engineering principles that are involved?
    In the previous course, I touched on the core data engineering principles,
    which fall largely into 4 buckets.
    Requirements, gathering, refinement, and sustainment.
    Requirements, briefly, is about identifying
    what you want to have in the data set.
    Gathering is about going out and actually figuring
    out and getting that data.
    Refinement is making sure that the data you've collected is, in fact, the data
    that you actually want.
    Sustainment is about improving the data to meet the various demands
    of the model over time.
    Let's talk about this very specifically in the context of speech
    commands, which Pete put together a couple of years ago.
    And let's talk about what requirement, gathering, refinement, and sustainment
    mean in the context of this particular TinyML data set.
    For requirements, Pete thought that it would
    be useful to have common use cases, like yes, no, and so forth.
    That's something that a data set should have.
    He also thought, well, robotics is on the rise,
    so it would be interesting to have words like left, right, go, stop,
    and so forth useful in the data set.
    He also thought numbers might be useful.
    0 through 9 would prove quite useful for general purpose applications.
    So those were the requirements that Pete went with.
    Then he went about the art of gathering all this data.
    Using crowdsourcing, he pulled together over 2,000
    some volunteers who consented to have their voices recorded and distributed.
    And they come from many different sources,
    so it's a rich data set, which has got many different accents in it.
    Luckily, that led to having over 1,000 samples for each of the keywords
    collected easily through a web based interface.
    Then came the refinement stage because not all of that data
    is actually useful.
    There are often words in there that were a little quiet.
    There were, in fact, wrong words.
    Someone was having a little bit of fun saying yes instead of no,
    saying no instead of yes, and so forth.
    So there had to be a process of actually cleaning up all of that data.
    Well, we can't sit around and actually manually clean the data.
    We need automated infrastructures and tools.
    For example, to remove low volume recordings because the network
    will not be able to pick them up.
    Or extract just a key portion of the word and not the entire sound
    that you might be collecting.
    For example, if there's a five second recording,
    well, within those 5 seconds, the keyword
    might actually just be a one second part in some snippet.
    So we have to cut out all the quiet noise.
    Then, of course, all of this needs to be validated in some sense.
    Luckily, with the help of crowdsourcing, we
    able to end up with over 100,000 utterances, which is great.
    All that said, you will have to do similar things when
    you start collecting your own data set.
    We'll talk about that more in the readings
    that come up that will supplement all the knowledge you need
    to have to collect a proper data set.
    Of course, even after you collect the data set,
    there's always the need to keep it evolving.
    You've got to meet new demographics, for instance.
    You've got to worry about biases.
    Who are the people who are actually going to use it?
    Do you have a nice balance between male and female voices?
    For example, in a previous course, I mentioned that in the Common Voice data
    set, for instance, they found that most of the recordings
    were male based recordings, and very few female voices were actually there
    in the data set, which can lead to a model that
    only tends to be more biased towards males, which is not a good thing.
    There's also diversity in age groups, the kind of accents,
    the dialects, the languages, and so forth.
    There are many different needs.
    So you have to understand where you're deploying the model,
    and then think about how you are going to sustain the data set.
    In other words, how are you going to keep evolving that data set?
    These are all great concepts to think and know about,

    Now after you have gone through the process of actually collecting

    You thought you collected the key word, but your microphone was not plugged in.
    You thought you collected the key word, but the microphone's volume

    Remember, background noise is very critical.
    We don't live in an isolated bubble in a noise proof environment.
    There's a lot of noise around us continuously that we are just
    used to picking up and ignoring.
    But we're going to have to teach our neural network
    to be able to do the exact same thing.

    does it work for everybody else?

    deploying your embedded system.

    you have recorded it, so the deployment environment is representative?
    Or are you actually deploying this in a noisy environment, like in a kitchen,
    for instance, where the family tends to often gather?
    Or perhaps in the car, while you're actually playing your background music?
    Or is it next to the TV or the radio, where
    there's a lot of background noise?

    Download Text (.txt) file    
    
    Another important concern when performing data engineering is data provenance. We must be cognizant of the origin of our data, most notably when we are using information procured from an external source, such as images from the internet. These images may be subject to copyright laws and other restrictions related to privacy. To preclude the possibility of legal issues, the origin of data should also be assessed, consulting with domain experts where necessary.
    
    Certain types of data may require labeling, which may be done using crowdsourcing platforms such as Amazon Mechanical Turk in some cases. However, in other cases, domain experts may be required to label data. For example, radiologists may be necessary to assess x-rays or mammograms for abnormalities that would be vague or unnoticeable to the layman.
    
    A further concern is the shifting of our objectives. If a plan is not sufficiently detailed or implemented correctly, dataset creep may occur, wherein more features are added in an attempt to improve the utility of the dataset. Alternatively, certain feature variables may be omitted or inputted incorrectly, such as with incorrect labeling. 
    
    Clearly, there are many ways in which the engineering of a dataset can go awry, and it is our job as a data engineer to ensure that the dataset has been properly collected with consideration of the deployment environment, as well as considerations of labeling, dataset bias, copyright, and other aforementioned concerns.
    
    In the remainder of this section, you will learn to design, collect and develop your own dataset for the creation of a custom keyword spotting model. Good luck!

\section{Things to Consider for your Data Collection Plan}

    As you make a plan to collect data for a custom keyword spotting application, there are several important data engineering questions to consider in designing your data collection and testing scheme:
    
\begin{itemize}
  \item Who are the anticipated end-users?
    \begin{itemize}
      \item What will the age range be?
      \item What languages will the user be speaking? Will the users have accents?
    \end{itemize}    
  \item What are the goals for using the application? How will this impact the requirements/needs of the ML model's performance?
    \begin{itemize}
      \item For example, will the user be trying to turn specific lights on/off vs. turn the thermostat up/down vs. arm/disarm a security system vs play your favorite playlist from a smart speaker, etc.?
    \end{itemize}    
  \item  What false positive or false negative rates can the application handle / what would the consequences be of such an event?
  \item In what environments will the users employ the application?
    \begin{itemize}
      \item How much background noise do we expect?
      \item How far will the users be from the device, and from noise sources in the environment?
      \item Will these values vary over time?
    \end{itemize}    
  \item    In what situations will the users use the application? Will the user’s speech sentiments vary?
    \begin{itemize}
      \item Will the user be stressed vs. calm vs. panicked?
      \item Will the user use different volumes of voice (whispered/normal/loud/shouted)?
      \item Will different users have different sentiments?
    \end{itemize}    
  \item   How do all of these previously mentioned factors affect how much and what kinds of target, non-target, and imposter/adversarial data you will need to train with?
    \begin{itemize}
      \item How likely is it that these wake words will be triggered unintentionally during a conversation?
      \item Who/how many people will be talking in the application environment?
    \end{itemize}    
  \item   Given all of these previously mentioned factors, what will you collect?
    \begin{itemize}
      \item What custom wake words will you select?
      \item Are the background noise samples from the Speech Commands dataset sufficient for model robustness? Would it be helpful to collect additional background noise samples from specific environments?
      \item Do you need to collect other words to ensure the model learns the difference between them and your wake words?
      \item How much data will you need to collect? We all know more is better (usually), but you also live in the real world with time constraints so how much do you think will be enough?
      \item In what environments will you collect these samples?
    \end{itemize}    
  \item   How will you test your model?
    \begin{itemize}
      \item What words will you try? How many times will you try these words? Will any of them be adversarial? Remember even current production systems struggle with close words at times!
      \item Who will test the model? How diverse will the testers be?
      \item Will the testers vary their speech sentiments?
      \item Where will the testers be in relation to the device?
      \item In what environments will the testing happen?
    \end{itemize}    
  \item Finally, based on your initial testing, how will you improve your results:
    \begin{itemize}
      \item What didn't work the first time?
      \item Will you need more or different data?
      \item How can you iteratively improve your results?
    \end{itemize}    
\end{itemize}    
         
\section{Your Data Collection Plan}

\begin{itemize}
    \item What is the goal of your KWS application going to be?
    
    \item How are you going to go about collecting your data (e.g., who, what, where, when)? 
    
    \item How many recordings are you going to do initially?
    
    \item Do you have a testing and revision plan?
    
    \item Do you have any suggestions for other students about their plans?
\end{itemize}            

\section{Building a Custom Dataset}

\subsection{Collecting a Custom Dataset for KWS}

NOTE: Our URL has changed from the video! See below for details!

There is another change from the video below -- please only record 10 words per recording session as Chrome will only allow you to download 10 files at a time. We are working to debug the zip library which will allow us to get back to ~30 files per recording session.

If you would like to run the open-speech-recording application locally (and therefore not have a limit on simultaneous downloads) please see our appendix document. Do note that you must be comfortable running python and python-pip from the command line to take this approach.

If you record using your own tools make sure to name your files keyword\_\#\#\#.ogg where keyword is whatever word you are recording and \#\#\# is any integer number. This is needed for the training scripts to process your files correctly.

We are going to use a modified version of Pete’s open-speech-recording project that will keep all of your recording data local to your browser (and thus it will never leave your computer)! You can open up the application in your browser by navigating to: \url{https://tinyml.seas.harvard.edu/open_speech_recording}
 NOTE: Our URL has changed from the video!


Please make sure to open this application on a device that you can easily download and upload files from (e.g., a laptop as compared to a phone).

If you see a warning that says: Your device does not support the HTML5 API needed to record audio (this is a known problem on iOS).

\begin{enumerate}
  \item If you are not using Chrome, we suggest you switch to Chrome. We have tested all of our applications on Chrome and cannot guarantee compatibility with other browsers.

  \item Make sure you are navigating to https://. For some reason, if you do not use the secure protocol, then the HTML 5 API will not work.

  \item Make sure you approve access to your microphone! The first time you load the webpage (or if you load it again in an incognito window), you should get a popup on the top left of your screen as shown below. Make sure to click the “allow” button!

  \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-6.1.png}
  
  A screenshot of the Chrome browser that has navigated to \url{https://tinymlx.org/open_speech_recording/} which has generated a warning popup which says “tinymlx.org wants to use your microphone” and below it has a button to “block” or to “allow.” This is where you have to click “allow” for recording to work. Please defer to the text for changes that have been made to the url and suggestions of how to use the webpage as it has evolved over time.

\end{enumerate}


\subsection{Using the Open Speech Recording App}

\begin{enumerate}
  \item In the middle of the screen, you will see the instructions for the App. Make sure you follow the steps in order. First, in step 1, type in as a comma separated list, the words you would like to record. And in step 2, type in as a single integer, the number of times you would like to record those words.

  Note that we have found that ~40 recordings each of 2-4 words often will be enough data to successfully train a moderately decent KWS application (i.e., it will work for your voice in whatever exact room you are in but may not generalize beyond that and may only work well for some of the keywords).

  Also, note that since all recordings will be saved in your browser memory and you can record multiple times by refreshing the page to reset the application, we do not suggest recording too many words in any one round of recording. We have found that Chrome will only let you download 10 recordings per session. So do not try to record more than 10 words before refreshing the page. Note that this is a change from the video as at the time of recording we were using a zip library that allowed ~30 words to be downloaded at a time. Unfortunately, that library, JSZip, now does not seem to be working. If you happen to be a web development guru and have ideas for a stable way to zip and download more files please do let us know!

  As a final note, remember that you can record both keywords and other distractor words (e.g., if your keyword was “slow” you could also record “snow”) to help. refine the model.

  \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-6.2-re.png}
  
  Another screenshot of the Open Speech Recording web page, this time showing the inputs to the UI. Namely step 1 says “Input the words you would like to record as a comma seperated list:” and in the input box below it has the example “These,are,example,words”. Step 2 says “Input the number of samples of each word you would like to record:” and the input below it has the example value 10. Please defer to the text for changes that have been made to the url and suggestions of how to use the webpage as it has evolved over time.

  \item Then, click the blue record button. You will see a 3,2,1 timer countdown occur and then recording will start.
  
   You will see a counter of X/Y appear that will show you your progress in recording all of the words. You will also see the current word you are supposed to record appear in the white box. The words will come shuffled in a random order, so sometimes they will alternate and sometimes, you will get the same word multiple times in a row. You will also see the waveform of your current speech appear in the grey box below that.

   It may take you a couple of tries to get the exact timing working. As a tip, the “Record” button will alternate between grey (as shown below) and red. Record the word shown in the white box each time the word record turns red.

   After each word is recorded, it will appear as a new row in the bottom of the screen.

  You can pause the recording session by clicking “Stop” at any time. The “Stop” button will then turn grey and the “Record” button will turn blue again. Click “Record” to restart the recording session.

  \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-6.3-re.png}
  
  Another screenshot of the Open Speech Recording web page, this time showing “record”, “stop”, and “download” buttons that appear in the middle of the page. Above those buttons is the wave form viewer that displays the sounds being recorded and above that is a counter showing (in this case) 1/40 and the word “words” which implies that this session is recording 40 words and the first word to be recorded is the word “words”. Below the buttons the recorded samples appear. Each has an audio play window that allows you to click play to hear the recording and a volume slider as well as a delete button to delete bad recordings as well as the label of what that recording's word is supposed to be. Please defer to the text for changes that have been made to the url and suggestions of how to use the webpage as it has evolved over time.

  \item Once you complete recording the final word in this session, a pop-up will appear asking if you’d like to download your recordings now. If you are satisfied, click “OK” otherwise, click “Cancel” and you can inspect the recordings one by one and download them later.

  \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-6.4.png}

  Another screenshot of the Open Speech Recording popup that appears once recording is done. It says “ tinymlx.org says Are you ready to download your data? If not, press cancel now, and then press Download once you are ready.” It then has a cancel and an OK button. Please defer to the text for changes that have been made to the url and suggestions of how to use the webpage as it has evolved over time.

  Note that if you use the application multiple times, you may get a pop-up asking for your permission to download multiple files. Click “Allow” and it should then automatically download all of your recordings again in the future without asking.

  \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-6.5.png}

  Another screenshot of the Chrome browser that has navigated to https://tinymlx.org/open\_speech\_recording/ which has generated a new warning popup which says “tinymlx.org wants to download multiple files” and below it has a button to “block” or to “allow.” This is where you have to click “allow” for downlaods to work. Please defer to the text for changes that have been made to the url and suggestions of how to use the webpage as it has evolved over time.

  \item To inspect a recording, simply press the play button on the left of its row. You can delete any recordings you don’t like by clicking the red “delete” button on the bottom right of its row. You can also adjust the volume of the recording as you listen to it by using the speaker icon on the right of each row.

  \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-6.6.png}

  Another screenshot of the Open Speech Recording web page, this time showing a zoom in on the previously mentioned audio play window that allows you to click play to hear the recording and a volume slider as well as a delete button to delete bad recordings as well as the label of what that recording's word is supposed to be. Please defer to the text for changes that have been made to the url and suggestions of how to use the webpage as it has evolved over time.

  \item Once you have removed any recordings, you do not like simply click the “Download” button in the middle of the screen that will have turned blue. Note the warning mentioned above about Chrome needing approval to download multiple items.

  \item And that’s it! You’ve successfully collected a KWS dataset. Feel free to record multiple times if you’d like to increase the size of your dataset and remember where your files are being downloaded. If you happen to be recording at a time where your download is a single zip file (instead of many .ogg audio files), you’ll want to unzip all of your recordings to a folder from which you can upload them into the next Colab for training! On most operating systems, right-clicking a zip file will reveal an option that says either “extract” or “open archive manager” or something along those lines. Clicking that will either immediately unzip the files or open up a window which will have an option to unzip the files.
\end{enumerate}


\section{Train and Deploy Your Custom Dataset KWS Model}

In this reading, we are going to walk through the steps for training a custom KWS model based on the dataset you just collected and then deploy that model onto your Arduino (including the necessary code changes in the Arduino IDE).
    
\subsection{Train and Deploy Your Custom Dataset KWS Model}

    0:00 / 0:00Klicken Sie auf die Pfeiltaste nach oben, um das Geschwindigkeitsmenu aufzurufen. Regeln Sie dann mit den Hoch und Runter Pfeiltasten die Geschwindigkeit. Klicken Sie Enter, um die jeweilige Geschwindigkeit festzulegen.Geschwindigkeit1.0xKlicke auf diesen Knopf und diess Video stumm oder nicht stumm zu schalten, oder drücke die HOCH oder RUNTER Knöpfe um die Lautstärke zu erhöhen oder abzusenken.Maximal Lautstärke.Videotranskript
    Beginn des Transkriptes, zum Ende springen.
    SPEAKER 1: All right, now that you've finished collecting your data set,
    it's time to train a model and then deploy it onto your Arduino.
    The Colab below will help you to take all of your existing recordings
    that you just did, and make sure that they're formatted correctly,
    so that the training script that we used in course two will work with them.
    You'll see, essentially, a copy of the assignment
    from keyword spotting when you retrained a model in course two.
    Paste it at the bottom of that Colab.
    We've kept the data set formatting and the training in the same Colab,
    so you don't have to upload the data set more than once.
    This will be especially important if you have a slow internet connection.
    Once you finish training your model, you'll see this cell at the bottom that
    runs that XXD step and will generate out your .cc model file.
    You can then take that model file and follow
