\subsection{Training with your Custom Dataset}

 The first thing you’ll need to do is to upload your custom dataset into Colab and train a KWS model. Then, in that Colab, we’ll need to convert that model first into a quantized .tflite file and then into a .cc file for use with the Arduino IDE.
    
 As with the pretrained model and favorite keyword model, we will be using the resulting .cc file, so make sure to download it or leave the tab open with the printout!
    
    \url{https://colab.research.google.com/github/tinyMLx/colabs/blob/master/4-6-8-CustomDatasetKWSModel.ipynb}
    
\subsection{Updating the Arduino Code}

\begin{enumerate}
  \item Again, open the micro\_speech.ino sketch, which you can find via the File drop-down menu. Navigate, as follows: File → Examples → Arduino\_TensorFlowLite →   micro\_speech.
  
  \item As before, navigate to the micro\_features\_model.cpp file and update the model.
    \begin{itemize}
      \item Copy the binary model file contents from the KWS\_custom.cc file into the micro\_features\_model.cpp file. As always, make sure to only copy the binary data inside the { } as the variable type is different in the downloaded or printed KWS\_custom.cc file.
      \item Next, scroll all the way down to the bottom of the file and replace the model length. Again, note that the .cpp file needs the variable to be of type const int while the .cc file will show unsigned int. Our suggestion again is to simply copy the numerical value.
    \end{itemize}
  \item Next, save your changes. Again, we suggest that you make a folder called e.g., TinyML inside of your Arduino folder. You can find your main Arduino folder either inside of your Documents folder or in your Home folder, and save it in that folder with a descriptive name like micro\_speech\_edx\_custom\_keywords. That said, you can save it wherever you like with whatever name you want!
 
  \item  The two other things we need to change are the count and list of keywords in the model settings files and the output response file. As we do that in the rest of   this document, we are going to assume that you used our model with the keywords “brian,” “vijay,” “pete.” If you choose to use different keywords, make sure   that you update the following steps accordingly.

  \item  Navigate to the micro\_features\_micro\_model\_settings.h file and scroll down to line 40 and change the value of the kCategoryCount variable to be two more than the number of keywords you selected (one extra for silence and one extra for unknown). For example, if you had 3 keywords (as we do in our example model), we need to update the variable to be 3 + 2 = 5 as shown below:
 
   \PYTHON{constexpr int kCategoryCount = 5;}
    
  \item  Then, navigate to the micro\_features\_micro\_model\_settings.cpp file. You’ll find that it includes the .h file and otherwise has a single array. Update the values of   that array to match your keywords. Make sure to leave “silence” and “unknown” in the list as well, and make sure to list your key words in the order   that you listed them on the training script. So, in the case of our example model, we would update the array to be:
  
  \begin{lstlisting}
   const char* kCategoryLabels[kCategoryCount] = {
        
        "silence",
        
        "unknown",
        
        "brian",
        
        "vijay",
        
        "pete",
        
    };
  \end{lstlisting} 
    
  \item  Finally, navigate to the arduino\_command\_responder.cpp file. Scroll down to line 54. There, you will see a series of if statements which control how the Arduino responds to each command. They are each structured to look at the value of the found\_command variable. This variable will be the string of the keyword (or “silence” or “unknown”) you entered into the kCategoryLabels array in the previous step. So, by comparing the [0] value in that string, the if statements are simply looking at the first letter! In each if statement you’ll also see a digitalWrite to either the LEDG, LEDR, or LEDB variable, which is used to turn on the appropriate color LED. So, if we wanted to adapt this to our three keywords and, for example, only turn on the single colors for our keywords and turn all of the colors on for unknown, which will come out look white, we could update those three if statements to the following:

  \begin{lstlisting}
    // Red for Vijay
    
    if(found_command[0] == 'v') {
        
        last_command_time = current_time;
        
        digitalWrite(LEDR, LOW);
        
    }
  \end{lstlisting} 
    
  \begin{lstlisting}
    // Green for Pete
    
    if(found_command[0] == 'p') {
        
        last_command_time = current_time;
        
        digitalWrite(LEDG, LOW);
        
    }
  \end{lstlisting} 
    
  \begin{lstlisting}
    // Blue for Brian -- note here that you do not need to index
    
    //                   into the first letter only, just a unique
    
    //                   letter combination in the keyword! That
    
    //                   said make sure you do not index beyond the
    
    //                   end of ANY keyword or you will get an error!
    
    if(found_command[0] == 'b' && found_command[1] == 'r') {
        
        last_command_time = current_time;
        
        digitalWrite(LEDB, LOW);
        
    }
  \end{lstlisting} 
    
  \begin{lstlisting}
    // All three for unknown (white)
    
    if(found_command[0] == 'u') {
        
        last_command_time = current_time;
        
        digitalWrite(LEDR, LOW);
        
        digitalWrite(LEDG, LOW);
        
        digitalWrite(LEDB, LOW);
        
    }
  \end{lstlisting} 

\end{enumerate}    
    
    Remember, if you used different keywords make sure to update the if statements accordingly. And that’s it! You’re Arduino code should be all set to handle your new model!
    
\subsection{Deploying the New Model}

\begin{enumerate}
  \item   Use a USB cable to connect the Arduino Nano 33 BLE Sense to your machine. You should see the green LED power indicator come on when the board first receives power.
  
  \item   As always, use the Tools drop-down menu to select appropriate Port and Board.
    \begin{itemize}
      \item Select the Arduino Nano 33 BLE as the board by going to Tools → Board: <Current Board Name> → Arduino Mbed OS Boards (nRF52840) → Arduino Nano 33 BLE.

      \item Then, select the USB Port associated with your board. This will appear differently on Windows, macOS, Linux but will likely indicate ‘Arduino Nano 33 BLE” in parenthesis. You can select this by going to Tools → Port: <Current Port (Board on Port)> → <TBD Based on OS> (Arduino Nano 33 BLE). Where <TBD Based on OS> is most likely to come from the list below where <\#> indicates some integer number.
        \begin{itemize}
          \item Windows → COM<\#>
          \item macOS → /dev/cu.usbmodem<\#>
          \item Linux → ttyUSB<\#> or ttyACM<\#>
        \end{itemize}
      \end{itemize}

  \item   Use the rightward arrow next to the ‘upload’ / flash the code. You’ll know the upload is complete when you see red text in the console at the bottom of the IDE that shows 100\% upload of the code and a statement that says something like “Done in <\#.\#> seconds.”

  \item If you receive an error, you will see an orange error bar appear and a red error message in the console (as shown below). Don’t worry -- there are many common reasons this may have occurred. To help you debug, please check out our FAQ appendix with answers to the most common errors!

  \item You should now have a working Keyword Spotting model trained on your own custom dataset deployed to your Arduino! To test it out, open the Serial Monitor. You should start to see the result of the model begin to display in the Serial Monitor and the LED’s light up according to how you instructed them in the previous section!
\end{enumerate}    
 
\section{How Well Does your Custom Dataset Model Work?}

How did your Custom Dataset Model work? Does it work for you in different locations? Does it work for any of your family and friends? How much data did you collect? Did you collect data from anyone other than yourself? If you made multiple models (or re-trained with additional data) did that improve performance? How well did your data collection plan actually work out?    


% 1.7: Visual Wake Words

\section{Recap: What are Visual Wake Words?}
    In this module, you will learn to build and implement an algorithm for detecting visual wake words on your local microcontroller.
    
    In the previous section focused on keyword spotting, we empowered our microcontroller with a lightweight auditory modality, allowing the system to monitor the microphone input in real-time to be able to recognize and respond to several keywords of interest to users. Visual wake words (VWW) are the natural extension of this technique to visual data, providing a lightweight visual modality to our microcontroller system.
    
    Image information is inherently more complex than auditory information to analyze using machine learning due to the higher dimensionality and complex spatial correlations. Consequently, image data requires greater computational and memory resources, making it challenging to deploy on resource-constrained microcontrollers. An additional complication, similar to keyword spotting, is the difficulty in procuring sufficient data to train our algorithm. In some scenarios, we may have to generate the dataset ourselves.
    
    In the previous classes, we have looked at the VWW task in some detail, from the implementation in Colab to discussions of image privacy and copyright. We analyzed a specific set of architectures, known as the MobileNet architectures, which leverage depthwise separable convolutions to minimize required memory and computational resources. We also looked at how transfer learning can be used to build upon pre-trained models that were used for similar tasks in the same domain to reduce our need to procure large datasets and train large deep learning models.
    
    The main VWW task we focused on was person detection, which focuses on determining the presence or absence of an individual in an image. In this task, we used the VWW dataset to train our person detection model and then performed transfer learning using a relatively small amount of images to adapt our model to perform mask detection. However, we have yet to demonstrate how to port and deploy such a model to our end-device, which will be the main focus of this section.
    
    By the end of this section, you should have a fully functioning VWW detection system. This shares some similarities to keyword spotting, but the deployment procedure will require some pre-and post-processing (e.g., image downscaling, encoding/decoding, and interfacing with the onboard camera) unique to the VWW detection task.
    
    Not only this, but we will also demonstrate the deployment of keyword spotting and VWW simultaneously using model “multitenancy.” More often than not, the typical focus is on running individual models in isolation, independent of other contextual activities. But increasingly there is a growing need to combine information from multiple sensors together to perform an intelligent action. To that end, we will learn how to perform audio-visual recognition, using both the microphone and camera. Our goal is to simultaneously operate two models at the same time in order to mimic sensor fusion (i.e., the ability to tie together inputs from different sensors in close temporal proximity).
    
    We will learn how to do this and more in TensorFlow Lite Micro.
    
\section{Person Detection Application Architecture}

    0:00 / 0:00Klicken Sie auf die Pfeiltaste nach oben, um das Geschwindigkeitsmenu aufzurufen. Regeln Sie dann mit den Hoch und Runter Pfeiltasten die Geschwindigkeit. Klicken Sie Enter, um die jeweilige Geschwindigkeit festzulegen.Geschwindigkeit1.0xKlicke auf diesen Knopf und diess Video stumm oder nicht stumm zu schalten, oder drücke die HOCH oder RUNTER Knöpfe um die Lautstärke zu erhöhen oder abzusenken.Maximal Lautstärke.Videotranskript
    Beginn des Transkriptes, zum Ende springen.
    VIJAY JANAPA REDDI: In this module, I'm very
    excited to be introducing you to a whole new application that
    involves the camera module.
    Specifically, we will be talking about person detection
    or sometimes referred to as the visual wakewards application.
    As I've done with the keyword spotting application,
    I will be talking about the overall application architecture.
    Let's first start off by talking about what the application is all about.
    Person detection is really simple.
    We want to know if there is a person in the camera's viewpoint or not.
    For example you are seeing Dylan Newton here and on the right hand side,
    you're not seeing him.
    So if there's a person, then we want to be notified that yes there is a person.
    If there is no person, I simply want to be notified that there is no person.
    This simple task has many diverse applications in the real world.
    For example, if you are deploying this sort of person detection in office
    buildings, you can use this to automatically turn
    lights on lights or off depending on the occupancy.
    That could be quite useful for conserving energy,
    especially in office buildings that are often large
    and consume a significant amount of electricity.
    Let's get started in understanding what's
    actually involved in putting in such an application together.
    There are two components as usual; there's initialization phase
    and there's a steady state main loop.
    The steady state main loop, has got multiple components
    involved the pre processing, the inference and the post-processing.
    Hopefully, by this point, you're quite comfortable with what those terms mean.
    And I'll step through them in detail soon.
    Let's get started with the initialization face.
    The initialization phase has got to subcomponents,
    you've got to initialize the camera and then you've
    got to set up things for your model.
    By and large, this breaks down into these smaller steps that
    are covered in a lot of detail, in the key word spotting application example,
    declaring variables all the way down to setting up
    things with the main loop, which is going to execute repeatedly.
    So I'm going to let things be at this point.
    Later when I do the screen cast, I'll talk about these in a bit more detail.
    Now, when you do the initialization, you've
    got initialize the camera, which is as simple as making this one
    single function call, camara.begin.
    As you can see here, this function call takes a couple of arguments.
    The first argument, QCIF, is what's called a Quarter Common Intermediate
    Format.
    That's a standard that means something in terms of the resolution.
    Second argument is GRAYSCALE.
    In order to do person detection, you do not need a color image.
    So we simply need a grayscale image that consumes less number of pixels
    and takes up less space.
    Third argument, is a number of frames per second.
    How often do we want the data coming in from the camera.
    And the final argument, is just a specific camera module itself.
    Which in our case is the OV7675.
    Then we move on to allocating the model in the initialization phase, which
    involves declaring the variable, setting up your interpreter,
    tying up the interpreter's input to where the buffer that
    is associated with the camera module.
    So the camera dumps the data into a buffer,
    that buffer has got to then get passed into the hands of like micro,
    because that's where the neural network is
    going to be seeding its inputs strong.
    And then you set things up for the main Loop.
    Pretty straightforward.
    Let's get into the pre processing part.
    The pre processing stage involves two components, which is,
    reading the image, cropping and converting.
    Reading the image involves simply invoking the function call that
    gets a frame of data from the OV7675.
    The data that you get back is in the format that I mentioned earlier, QCIF.
    QCIF has a precise resolution that's associated
    with it, which is 176 in number of columns
    and 144 in terms of the number of rows.
    This data with this function call, will end up on [INAUDIBLE] system.
    The next part, before we can actually go into the inference stage,
    is really cropping and converting the model from QCIF,
    into a format that's rendered for our actual neural network.
    In this case, it is about scaling that original QCIF image, into a 96
    by 96 square pixel.
    This can easily be done through this little function
    that we have already provided for you, which I will talk about more in detail,
    let me do the screen test.
    But it's very simple, is the key takeaway here.
    You're cropping the image down in size and then
    you're converting it into a format that's ready for the neural network
    to consume.
    Given that, once we have the input data that's ready,
    we simply invoke the model.
    In our case, the model that we're invoking here,
    is a mobile nets model, that actually runs
    on top of the image to detect whether a person is there or not.
    And in order to invoke the model, this is all we do.
    At this point you've got to be quite comfortable with actually
    looking at this APIs knowing exactly what's happening.
    The next step, is figuring out what needs to happen
    during the post processing phase.
    Remember the post processing phase is all about understanding
    what to do with the data that has come out of the neural network.
    So in our application, it's all about responding to
    whether a person was detected or not, which
    we are going to do simply through the LEDs that
    are present on our embedded system.
    So for example, if there is a person in the view,
    then we simply want to light that green LED,
    if there is no person in the viewpoint, then we simply
    want to light the red LED.
    And that's all there is.
    So hopefully one of the key things that you're seeing now,
    is that compared to keyword spotting.
    The visual wakewards to the person detection example,
    is remarkably simple.
    So not every application has the same level of complexity
    in terms of the initialization, in terms of the preprocessing,
    in terms of inference and in terms of the post-processing.
    So just keep that in mind.
    One of the thing you should probably think about,
    is the fact that the stages tend to stay the same.
    So there's a certain pattern that repeats
    in terms of how you want to process.
    And if you want to look for something that you want to change,
    keep that in mind.
    Just as I said to do the same in the context
    of the keyword spotting application.
    With that said, let's get a little more creative
    in terms of how we can actually deploy an interesting application using
    the camera module, which incorporates person detection,
    but in addition it'll actually incorporate
    getting another sensor, which makes it much more exciting, more on that soon.
    Ende des Transkriptes, zum Anfang springen
    Downloads und Transskripte

\section{Person Detection Screencast}

    0:00 / 0:00Klicken Sie auf die Pfeiltaste nach oben, um das Geschwindigkeitsmenu aufzurufen. Regeln Sie dann mit den Hoch und Runter Pfeiltasten die Geschwindigkeit. Klicken Sie Enter, um die jeweilige Geschwindigkeit festzulegen.Geschwindigkeit1.0xKlicke auf diesen Knopf und diess Video stumm oder nicht stumm zu schalten, oder drücke die HOCH oder RUNTER Knöpfe um die Lautstärke zu erhöhen oder abzusenken.Maximal Lautstärke.Videotranskript
    Beginn des Transkriptes, zum Ende springen.
    VIJAY JANAPA REDDI: In this screencast, I'm
    going to walk you through the person detection example.
    Specifically, I'm going to walk you through the entire application
    architecture, starting with the initialization phase
    and then stepping through the various components of the main loop.
    Now, one of the things that I'm going to do differently
    in this particular screencast is that I'm not going to break down
    the screencast into multiple screen casts
    to explain each one of the stages.
    That is the initialization, preprocessing, inference,
    and postprocessing, and different levels.
    The reason is because it's a fairly simple example.
    Moreover, I want to help you see the connections in a single screencast
    so you can feel confident that you understand
    the general template that any TinyML application is going to follow.
    So let's start off with the initialization step,
    which you may recall has got a whole bunch of small steps involved.
    So let's get started with that.
    The first step is to declare your variables.
    As you can see here from line 30, we're declaring the variables.
    The first chunk of variable declarations should
    look quite familiar to what we did in keyword spotting.
    We declare a handle to the error reporter,
    a handle to the model that we'll be loading,
    a handle to the microinterpreter, and a handle to the input
    tensor that will be used as the source for doing the inference.
    And the second portion is really simply declaring the tensor arena
    and allocating some memory for it.
    Once we're done with this, we get into the next step of loading the model.
    Now that is actually done inside the setup routine, which is right here.
    So as you can see here, we're going in and getting the model.
    G\_person\_detect\_model\_data is the file that actually
    contains the actual neural network.
    That is actually held in here, which if I scroll to the top, you can see
    is g\_person\_detect\_model\_data, which is an array which has
    got the neural network architecture.
    Going back here, we do a quick error check
    to make sure that the schema of the model
    actually matches the schema of what we're
    expecting in this particular version of TensorFlow Lite Micro.
    Following that, we have to resolve the operators
    and that's what we're doing here.
    We've got five operators that we will be using.
    Each one of those includes the AveragePool2D, the Conv2D,
    the DepthWiseConv2D, the m and the Softmax.
    Alternatively, we could have used AllOpsResolver, which we are not
    because we want to conserve the memory.
    After initializing the OpsResolver, our next step
    is to actually initialize the interpreter using all the variables we
    have set up, such as what is the model file, what's the ops that we'll
    be using, what's the tensor arena, what's its size,
    and how do we report errors.
    Finally, we will then allocate the raw memory that's
    required for the tensor arena, and then we
    will set up the input buffer for the neural network.
    And that's all there is for the initialization phase.
    This should look very similar to what you did with keyword spotting.
    The key takeaway here that this, there is a certain template that repeats over
    and over regardless of what the application is.
    Keep that in mind.
    It will help you make forward progress much faster.
    Now we're going to get into the actual main loop of the application.
    Remember, the main loop has preprocessing, inference,
    and postprocessing.
    So that is broken down here into three simple parts.
    The first part is the preprocessing, where
    the job is simply to get an image.
    That means you've got to read an image, you've got a crop and convert it.
    Both those are tucked away nicely inside GetImage.
    After you've finished the preprocessing, we
    do the inference, which is simply through this function call.
    After that, we do the postprocessing, where
    we process the data that's coming out of the neural network.
    Let's go through each of these stages in detail.
    Let's go and see what's happening inside GetImage.
    The job of GetImage is quite simple.
    We want to get an image from the camera module.
    If this is the very first time we're calling the camera module,
    then we're going to initialize it.
    As you recall from the slides I showed you,
    the camera module uses a QCIF format, requesting pixels in grayscale
    at five frames per second for this camera module, which
    we have interfaced with the TinyML [? shield. ?]
    This is only done the very first time.
    Subsequently, we read the data, and after that, we
    crop and scale the image down into a resolution that
    makes sense for our particular neural network, which is 96 by 96 pixels.
    After we are done with the preprocessing,
    we then get into the inference stage, and the inference stage is very simple.
    We simply call the interpreter, which gets its input data from the input
    buffer.
    After the neural network is run, then we get into the postprocessing stage
    where we get the output from the neural network, which is a tensor,
    and then we look at the Softmax probabilities
    for its outputs, which are in the form of a person's score or no person score.
    We pass that off to the responder.
    The responder's job is to light an LED depending on
    whether it detects a person or not.
    So let's go ahead and look at what's actually
    happening inside RespondToDetection.
    This function, which is inside the Arduino detection responder,
    performs a couple of small bookkeeping tasks
    here, which I will let you read at this point
    because you should be familiar with the code.
    The key nugget of this particular function is really this part.
    If it thinks that the probability--
    "it" being the neural network-- if the neural network guessed
    that the probability that the person actually is in the picture
    is greater than the probability that there's no person in the picture,
    then we will ask the green LED to turn on by writing digitalWrite
    as a LOW on the LEDG.
    That turns on the green LED.
    Or, vice versa.
    If there is no person, then it will turn on the red LED.
    And that is pretty much all there is to the postprocessing stage.
    Again, once this is done, we go back to the start of the loop.
    We go through the preprocessing stage, we run the inference,
    then we run the preprocessing.
    What I'm trying to get you to realize here
    is the same general structure of initialization followed
    by main loop coupled with preprocessing, inference,
    and postprocessing inside the main loop is the overall pipeline
    that we'll be rinsing and repeating regardless
    of what application you're doing.
    And that is the key takeaway that I want you to have
    from this particular screencast.

\section{Person Detection with Keyword Spotting: MultiModal}

    0:00 / 0:00Klicken Sie auf die Pfeiltaste nach oben, um das Geschwindigkeitsmenu aufzurufen. Regeln Sie dann mit den Hoch und Runter Pfeiltasten die Geschwindigkeit. Klicken Sie Enter, um die jeweilige Geschwindigkeit festzulegen.Geschwindigkeit1.0xKlicke auf diesen Knopf und diess Video stumm oder nicht stumm zu schalten, oder drücke die HOCH oder RUNTER Knöpfe um die Lautstärke zu erhöhen oder abzusenken.Maximal Lautstärke.Videotranskript
    Beginn des Transkriptes, zum Ende springen.
    VIJAY JANAPA REDDI: As we get ready to deploy the person detection example,
    I want us to kind of reflect back on the way we have been
    thinking about the usage of sensors.
    Specifically, when I talked about keyword spotting,
    I only mentioned the microphone.
    When I talked about the person detection example,
    I only mentioned the camera module.
    But is there something interesting that we
    can do if we combined multiple sensors together to build richer applications?
    For example, can our personal detection example
    be richer if we use the microphone data as well?
    The answer is absolutely yes.
    For instance, if we combined the visual input with the audio input,
    we will be able to predict moods, which is quite powerful.
    Now, how would we go about this?
    This is where the concept of multimodal comes in.
    Multimodal means combining input that's coming in from multiple sensors
    into a single classification, and there are loads of benefits around this.
    Specifically, they fall into two categories, which
    is called complementary or redundancy.
    Redundancy means there are multiple sources of the same information,
    such as lip movement and parts of speech,
    that can be used in conjunction with one another
    to increase the reliability and the prediction accuracy of the model.
    Complementary means that we can detect things through data fusion
    that we would have otherwise not been able to detect
    if we were only using a single sensor.
    So this sounds like a really awesome thing to be able to do.
    We all want multimodal because if we have multimodal, then
    we might be able to build really interesting applications.
    How do we go about doing that?
    Well, let's go back to the ML workflow.
    It all starts with the data set.
    If you want to be able to do multimodal, that
    means we need to have multiple streams of input data.
    So here, we need to have a visual stream for the facial expressions
    and then we need to have an audio stream.
    Now, these are data sets, basically, but these data sets need to be aligned.
    For example, in this first figure, the person has a neutral expression
    and there's a set of words that are associated
    with that neutral expression.
    This person here is happy and there's something
    that this person is saying at that particular point that
    is aligned with the happy feeling.
    Here, the person looks sad, so they're probably saying something
    that's associated with a sad word.
    And here, they seem excited, which means that there's probably something
    that they're saying that is actually associated with the excitement.
    Now, these things have to be perfectly aligned
    for us to be able to train the model.
    Now, let's assume that we actually have such a wonderful data set where there's
    perfect alignment between the two different input sensors,
    because multimodal tends to work best for tasks
    where each sensor provides context for a single classification.
    For example, predicting a user's mood based
    on their voice and facial expression.
    So let's assume that we have this data set,
    then how would we actually go about training the model?
    Well, we have to effectively choose an embedding in order to train the model.
    Now, the embedding has to be a joint representation that
    comes in both from the camera stream as well as the audio stream.
    So for images that are coming in from the camera,
    we would have an image encoder that spits out an encoding that
    corresponds to the image.
    Similarly, we would have an encoding that corresponds to the audio input.
    Now, these encoders can be trained separately
    and their output is effectively concatenated and then
    used to train the multimodal classifier.
    Now, let's assume that we know how to do this and everything goes well.
    When it comes to the evaluation conversion process,
    it's exactly the same as what we have done before.
    Now comes the deployment phase.
    Wow, we are very excited about deploying a multimodal network
    on our embedded device.
    Wouldn't that be exciting for person detection, where we were
    able to tell the mood of the person?
    Let's talk about some of the complexities
    that we're going to run into because there
    are some gotchas here where there's a fundamental decoupling
    between the deployment stage and the rest of the pipeline,
    and that's extremely important to understand because this does happen
    in production scenarios where the people who are training the model
    do not realize how the model is going to be deployed
    and that leads into a fundamental mismatch that breaks the application
    use case.
    The complication with deployment really comes
    about as a result of having to deal with synchronizing the input
    buffers from multiple sensors.
    The camera capture is happening at five [? fips ?] at a steady state,
    but the actual speed really depends on the inference latency
    on the embedded microcontroller.
    On the Adreno, one iteration of the whole person detection loop
    takes 740 milliseconds for us.
    And the keyword spotting audio track filled up the one second buffer
    and the amount of data there depends on how long
    it has been since the last inference that was performed
    on the keyword spotting pipeline.
    In either case, if we take too long between the inferences,
    we're going to miss proper alignment for the keyword.
    Which means even though we have this network that
    has been trained with aligned data and actually
    is capable of multimodal execution, the problem you're going to have
    is that the inputs, when they're coming in on the embedded system,
    if they are off and they are not aligned,
    then the network is going to have missed predictions
    and therefore it would simply not work.
    Now, that puts us in a bit of a pickle because this sounds like something
    that we would ideally like to have, but is there
    something that we can do in order to unlock
    this sort of an interesting application use case
    without having to deal with the issues that we are just learning about,
    which is the issue of having to deal with the synchronization
    between the sensors on the deployment aspect of it?
    On that note, I'm going to stop here and I'll
    come back and talk about what I call is multitenancy, which
    gets us a step closer to being able to realize
    the utilization of multiple sensors to unlock
    these kinds of creative applications where
    we can benefit from having rich input coming
    in from multiple sensors in order to build an interesting application.
    So I'll see you there in the next video.
    Ende des Transkriptes, zum Anfang springen

\section{Person Detection with Keyword Spotting: Multi-Tenancy}

    0:00 / 0:00Klicken Sie auf die Pfeiltaste nach oben, um das Geschwindigkeitsmenu aufzurufen. Regeln Sie dann mit den Hoch und Runter Pfeiltasten die Geschwindigkeit. Klicken Sie Enter, um die jeweilige Geschwindigkeit festzulegen.Geschwindigkeit1.0xKlicke auf diesen Knopf und diess Video stumm oder nicht stumm zu schalten, oder drücke die HOCH oder RUNTER Knöpfe um die Lautstärke zu erhöhen oder abzusenken.Maximal Lautstärke.Videotranskript
    Beginn des Transkriptes, zum Ende springen.
    VIJAY JANAPA REDDI: In this video, I'm going
    to introduce the concept of multi-tenancy, which
    is different than multimodal execution.
    Multi-tenancy is a proxy way of getting close
    to some aspects of multimodal execution.
    Remember, in multimodal execution, what we wanted to do
    was that we wanted to take the inputs that are coming from multiple sensors
    and fuse them together into a combined encoding
    and feed that into a single neural network
    in order to perform a classification.
    However, when it comes to deployment, due
    to rate mismatches between the input sensors,
    be it the microphone or the camera or whatever your favorite sensor is,
    there's a high probability that the data is not going to be aligned.
    Now, if the input data coming into your trained neural network is not aligned,
    then the network might not be able to recognize the things
    that it was originally trained on.
    Now, remember, during the training phase in a multimodal network,
    the data is nicely aligned.
    However, when you're deploying things onto an embedded microcontroller
    where there are many different kinds of sensors
    and there are many different kinds of platforms on which you can deploy,
    and that heterogeneity can potentially lead into problems where
    the rate at which the input data is coming in from one sensor
    might not match the rate at which the other sensor is coming in at.
    And that can lead to failure problems.
    I'm not saying that this is not something that we can never realize.
    I'm just saying that this often tends to happen.
    However, there are ways to get around this.
    Now, let me take a very specific application
    to introduce the concept of multi-tenancy.
    Let's say we want to build a contact-free elevator, where the goal
    is to enforce mask wearing at offices.
    This will fundamentally require us to build two types of neural networks
    that work together.
    Because we're saying contact-free, well, then there
    has to be some way for the user to communicate
    which floor they want to go to.
    Maybe the keywords here are level one, level two, level three, level four,
    and so forth.
    And now we also want the system to enforce mask wearing.
    In other words, we wanted to open the door only
    when it recognizes that the person is wearing a mask.
    Well, in that particular case, after it's received the keyword,
    it would have to check to see if the person is actually wearing a mask
    and then allow the doors to open.
    Now, how would we do this?
    Well, in many ways, you've already been exposed to all the fundamental building
    blocks to enable you to do this.
    We want to run two separate models on the same system, effectively
    at the same time.
    You want the camera module to be running to look
    for masks, which we did in course two.
    And you want the keyword spotting pipeline
    to be running in order to detect the keyword, which you've already done
    and, in fact, gone to the extent of deploying on your microcontroller.
    Now, as these two networks run in parallel, in multi-tenancy,
    you effectively combine the outputs that are coming out of the networks
    into a decision-making process.
    Now let's look at what this means from a deployment pipeline
    sort of perspective.
    If you look at the machine learning workflow for multi-tenancy,
    we don't have to go all the way back to collecting the data.
    Remember, in multimodal, we actually have to go all the way back
    to the data set part.
    And we have to make sure we have data sets
    where things are actually perfectly aligned so that we can train a model
    and then be able to deploy it.
    In the multi-tenancy perspective, you don't have that problem.
    Because you're running the networks independently,
    there is no need for the data to be aligned.
    As such, the left-hand part of the entire pipeline can stay intact
    and you can reuse whatever you have done.
    Now, when it comes to the deployment stage, what we would do
    is that we would cascade the neural network architectures together
    in order to make a combined decision.
    For example, here, you would take the audio that's
    coming in from the microphone, make a decision
    on whether the input is yes or no, and then you would feed that,
    if it is a yes, into the camera pipeline,
    and say that, well, what is the camera saying?
    If the keyword is detected and the camera
    says that the person is wearing a mask, voila, then you can open the doors.
    So hopefully what you're learning here is
    that you can run more than one model.
    Very often when people talk about [INAUDIBLE] applications,
    they tend to deal with a single sensor.
    And what I'm trying to do here is to introduce you to more than that.
    I'm trying to get you to think beyond just a single sensor at a time.
    Hopefully you get that concept.
    And in the next video, what I'm going to do
    is I'm going to show you some of the nuances in the deployment stage
    about deploying the multi-tenancy effectively.
    And I'll see you there.
    Ende des Transkriptes, zum Anfang springen

 \section{Multi Tenancy in TensorFlow Lite Micro}
 
     0:00 / 0:00Klicken Sie auf die Pfeiltaste nach oben, um das Geschwindigkeitsmenu aufzurufen. Regeln Sie dann mit den Hoch und Runter Pfeiltasten die Geschwindigkeit. Klicken Sie Enter, um die jeweilige Geschwindigkeit festzulegen.Geschwindigkeit1.0xKlicke auf diesen Knopf und diess Video stumm oder nicht stumm zu schalten, oder drücke die HOCH oder RUNTER Knöpfe um die Lautstärke zu erhöhen oder abzusenken.Maximal Lautstärke.Videotranskript
     Beginn des Transkriptes, zum Ende springen.
     SPEAKER 1: In this video, I want to talk about how
     you achieve Multi Tenancy in TensorFlow Lite Micro
     so that you can effectively deploy two neural networks on the device,
     at the same time, to be able to achieve something close to a [? multimodal ?]
     experience.
     Now how would we go about that, and what are
     some of the issues that will come up, are the things
     that I want to talk to you about.
     Remember that we want to deploy two models.
     We want to deploy a vision model and we want to deploy an audio model.
     Now, whenever you're deploying things, a lot of the problems
     will really come down to the amount of memory you actually have.
     We have SRAM and we have Flash.
     The vision model takes up some amount of SRAM and Flash, as shown here.
     Then you have the audio model, which in addition to the vision model,
     will take up a bit more space.
     Now, whether this is a problem or not really
     depends on the device's limit for both the SRAM and the Flash.
     In this particular situation, the SRAM proves
     to be problematic because you only have 256 kilobytes,
     but your combined usage of memory for the audio and vision processing
     is going to exceed that, which means we will have a problem during execution
     time.
     On Flash, this is not a problem.
     Our one megabyte of available storage does not
     seem to be a concern because the vision and audio models
     are not exceeding that device limit.
     So let's try and understand what the problem really is with the SRAM.
     For any given model, be it the vision model or the audio model,
     there are two parts.
     There is the persistent part, and there's the temporary part.
     Think of the temporary as all the temporary storage
     that is required for the input feature, maps, and activations, and so forth.
     The persistent part of the part that will
     correspond to the actual weights of the network, for instance.
     Now, these two combined together-- that is,
     the vision model's temporary plus the vision model's persistent part,
     in addition to the audio model's temporary and the audio model's
     persistent part-- is what is causing us to exceed the SRAM that we actually
     have available on our device.
     Now is there something we can do here?
     Well, there is in fact a trick that you can
     play when you're implementing your code in TensorFlow Lite Micro, which
     is, you can combine the memory usage for the audio model's temporary state
     and the vision model's temporary state.
     By combining them, you reduce the total amount of memory that is being used.
     Because the models are not effectively going to be running concurrently,
     what we can do is that we can actually get the memory
     to be reused across the models.
     Now, I said that the models are not running concurrently.
     Keep in mind here, that we are implementing a cascade architecture.
     A cascade architecture means you have one model that runs first.
     And then based on its input, you might trigger a second model.
     Now because of that, the two models are effectively not competing
     for resources at the same time so you would actually
     be able to do a little bit of optimization here.
     Now, I just want to give you a quick view of what the code op
     changes are going to look like.
     In a standard allocator, for example, what we
     would have is the above definition shown on the right.
     We would have a tensor arena that is set up for the visual [INAUDIBLE] model,
     for the person detector.
     And then, when we look at the audio for the keyword spotting model,
     we would have an associated amount of keyword spotting tensor
     arena dedicated for it.
     And then we would create an interpreter that
     corresponds to the keyword spotting, and then we would allocate its own tensors.
     And we're doing the exact same thing for the vision model up on top.
     Now because of this, we're exceeding the amount of memory allocation
     that's available on the SRAM.
     Now with a shared allocator, however, what we are doing is
     we're actually creating a combined tensor arena,
     where the total amount of memory is actually
     going to be much less in total usage.
     The reason is because again, when we create interpreters
     to run for the audio model and the vision model,
     they're actually running back to back in the cascade style.
     And therefore, they're not going to be competing for the temporary storage,
     and that is something that we can actually reuse.
     And to that end, TensorFlow Lite Micro has actually
     got APIs that allow us to do this very effectively, as shown on the right.
     And we'll talk about this in a bit more detail later on.
     But specifically, the key takeaway has got
     to be that TensorFlow Lite Micro allows us to implement a shared allocator that
     will reuse temporary allocations between models, which helps us conserve space.
     And in doing so, we have the ability now to support Multi Tenancy in execution.
     And this means that we can enable the rich kind of applications
     that we started talking about in the person detection use case.
     With that said, later on, Brian is going to show you
     how we're going to deploy a Multi Tenancy model where the goal is
     to actually be able to use the keyword spotting model that you know,
     and to couple it with the person detection model, which
     is getting introduced to you for the first time now.
     And together, we're not only learning to use the camera model,
     but we're actually unlocking a whole new level of TinyML application space.
     Have fun, and I will see you later.
     Ende des Transkriptes, zum Anfang springen
     Downloads und Transskripte
     Video
     Videodatei herunterladen
     Transskripte
     Download SubRip (.srt) file
     Download Text (.txt) file   
    
\section{Deploying the Pretrained Person Detection Model}

    In this reading, you will deploy the pretrained person detection model onto your Arduino and walk through a little bit of the code to better understand how it works.
    
\subsection{Deploying the Pretrained Person Detection Model}

    0:00 / 0:00Klicken Sie auf die Pfeiltaste nach oben, um das Geschwindigkeitsmenu aufzurufen. Regeln Sie dann mit den Hoch und Runter Pfeiltasten die Geschwindigkeit. Klicken Sie Enter, um die jeweilige Geschwindigkeit festzulegen.Geschwindigkeit1.0xKlicke auf diesen Knopf und diess Video stumm oder nicht stumm zu schalten, oder drücke die HOCH oder RUNTER Knöpfe um die Lautstärke zu erhöhen oder abzusenken.Maximal Lautstärke.Videotranskript
    Beginn des Transkriptes, zum Ende springen.
    BRIAN PLANCHER: Hi, everyone.
    In this reading we're going to be deploying the stock person detection
    example.
    You can find that example by going to the Harvard Tiny MLX library
    and opening up the person detection example.
    We won't be making any changes to the person detection example,
    so we can just simply deploy it.
    Check the board and check the port as always,
    and then you can just click Upload and deploy it onto your device.
    Now that you've gotten the model deployed,
    let's open up the serial monitor.
    In the serial monitor, you're going to see a series
    of person score and no person score.
    And what this is indicating with each integer value,
    is how strongly it believes that there is a person or not
    a person in the image.
    And so negative numbers are bad positive, numbers are good.
    And so what you're seeing, is right now it believes
    there is not a person in the image.
    And if we look at the Arduino, we can see that the light is red
    and that confirms that.
    Now you see these little blue flashes, and the blue light
    flashes every time the camera takes a picture and it does a single inference.
    Now, if I prop this up here on the box and I look down at it from a little bit
    farther away, you can see that I can turn the light green.
    And that's because I'm now far enough and in the right field of view
    that the camera can see my face and it now thinks there's a person.
    And if we look in the serial monitor, you
    can see that the person score is now positive,
    while the no person score is negative.
    And that's it.
    That's how the person detection example works.
    Ende des Transkriptes, zum Anfang springen
    Downloads und Transskripte
    Video
    Videodatei herunterladen
    Transskripte
    Download SubRip (.srt) file
    Download Text (.txt) file
    
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Use a USB cable to connect the Arduino Nano 33 BLE Sense to your machine. You should see the green LED power indicator come on when the board first receives power.
  
  \item  Open the person\_detection.ino sketch, which you can find via the File drop-down menu. Navigate, as follows: File → Examples → Harvard\_TinyMLx→ person detection. Our library version of the example is very similar to the one you’ll find in the TensorFlow Lite for Microcontrolers library, but includes a couple of important tweaks to work better with the TinyML Shield and both the OV7675 and OV7670 so make sure you open the example out of our library.

  \item  Use the Tools drop-down menu to select the appropriate Port and Board. This is important as it is telling the IDE which board files to use and on which serial connection it should send the code. In some cases, this may happen automatically, but if not, you’ll want to select: 

    \begin{itemize}
      \item Select the Arduino Nano 33 BLE as the board by going to Tools → Board: <Current Board Name> → Arduino Mbed OS Boards (nRF52840) → Arduino Nano 33 BLE. Note that on different operating systems the exact name of the board may vary but/and it should include the word Nano at a minimum. If you do not see that as an option, then please go back to Setting up the Software and make sure you have installed the necessary board files.
      
      \item Then, select the USB Port associated with your board. This will appear differently on Windows, macOS, Linux, but will likely indicate ‘Arduino Nano 33 BLE” in parenthesis. You can select this by going to Tools → Port: <Current Port (Board on Port)> → <TBD Based on OS> (Arduino Nano 33 BLE). Where <TBD Based on OS> is most likely to come from the list below where <\#> indicates some integer number:
        \begin{itemize}
          \item Windows → COM<\#>
          \item macOS → /dev/cu.usbmodem<\#>
          \item Linux → ttyUSB<\#> or ttyACM<\#>
        \end{itemize}
      \end{itemize}

  \item   Use the rightward arrow to upload / flash the code. Do not be alarmed if you see a series of orange warnings appear in the console. This is expected as we are working with bleeding edge code. You’ll know the upload is complete when you red text in the console at the bottom of the IDE that shows 100% upload of the code and a statement that says something like “Done in <#.#> seconds.”
    
    If you have the OV7670 camera, make sure to first make the changes indicated at the end of this document!
    
    If you receive an error, you will see an orange error bar appear and a red error message in the console. Don’t worry -- there are many common reasons this may have occurred.
    
    To help you debug other issues, please check out our FAQ appendix with answers to the most common errors!
    
  \item    Open up the serial monitor. You will start to see it print a series of messages about the person score and the no person score. This is indicating the model’s confidence in whether there is either a person or no person in the image!

  \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-7.png}

    Screenshot of the Serial Monitor showing a series of Person vs. No Person scores. We see that the values can be either positive or negative integers. This is indicating the confidence that the model thinks and image is either a person or not a person. We see it start with positive values for person and negative values for not a person and then switch. This is because Brian started in view of the camera and then moved.

    At this point, you’ll want to look at the board itself. The LED should be flashing blue when an image is being captured and then either red or green. Green indicates that the model detects that there is a person in the image and red indicates that it does not detect a person. Try holding the Arduino at arm's length and point it toward your face. The LED should flash green. As you may see, the field of view is very narrow, so if it’s not working, try propping up the Arduino and standing further back.
    
  \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-7.2}% .gif}
    
    A GIF of the board LED turning from red to green as Brian offscreen angles it at his face by propping it up on the box.
\end{enumerate}    


\subsection{Understanding the Code in the Person Detection Example}

    Now that you have gotten the person detection application deployed to your microcontroller, let's explore the code a little bit. The main person\_detection.ino file consists of the standard Arduino setup() and loop() functions. These are both quite similar to the hello\_world and micro\_speech example. To really start to understand the code, let's take a look at a couple of the other files.
    
    Open the arduino\_image\_provider.cpp file. Here, we will find the GetImage() function, which captures an image and loads it into the model’s input.
    
    First, let’s take a look at the camera initialization. Here, we set the resolution of the image we receive from the camera to QCIF (176x144). In order to save space and complexity, the input to our model is a grayscale image, therefore, we set the camera to transmit a grayscale image. We also set our camera model to the OV7675.
    
    Note: As shown in the changes to the OV7670 section below, this is where you’ll need to change the OV7675 to OV7670.
    
  \begin{lstlisting}
    // Initialize camera if necessary
    
    if (!g_is_camera_initialized) {
        
        if (!Camera.begin(QCIF, GRAYSCALE, 5, OV7675)) {
            
            TF_LITE_REPORT_ERROR(error_reporter, "Failed to initialize camera!");
            
            return kTfLiteError;
            
        }
        
        g_is_camera_initialized = true;
        
    }
  \end{lstlisting}    
    
    Next, we can capture an image with Camera.readFrame(). Since the input of our model is a 96x96 image, we have to crop the 176x144 image we receive from the camera before passing it to the model. To do this, we copy over the desired pixel values from the center of our original image into the input vector of our model.
    
  \begin{lstlisting}
    // Crop 96x96 image. This lowers FOV, ideally we would downsample
    
    // but this is simpler and more efficient on device.
    
    for (int y = min_y; y < min_y + 96; y++) {
        
        for (int x = min_x; x < min_x + 96; x++) {
            
            // convert TF input image to signed 8-bit
            
            image_data[index++] = static_cast<int8_t>(data[(y * 176) + x] - 128);
            
        }
        
    }
  \end{lstlisting}    
    
    Now that we are familiar with capturing and loading the image, let’s take a look at how the person detection application handles the output of the model. To do that, open the arduino\_detection\_responder.cpp file.
    
    The primary function of the detection responder is to control the LED output. In a real application, this function would drive some other core function that is triggered by the presence of a person.
    
    The blue LED will flash every inference, demonstrating how often an inference is run. Then we will compare the person no\_person scores that are the outputs of the model. In this case, we flash the green LED when the person\_score is higher than the no\_person\_score (both of which are reported over serial). In some applications, we might be more concerned with the false positives or false negatives, in which case, we can adjust the criteria for detecting a person, potentially by using a threshold as we have discussed in Course 2.
    
  \begin{lstlisting}
    //Flash the blue LED after every inference.
    
    digitalWrite(LEDB, LOW);
    
    delay(100);
    
    digitalWrite(LEDB, HIGH);
  \end{lstlisting}    
    
  \begin{lstlisting}
    // Switch on the green LED when a person is detected,
    
    // the red when no person is detected
    
    if (person_score > no_person_score) {
        
        digitalWrite(LEDG, LOW);
        
        digitalWrite(LEDR, HIGH);
        
    }
    
    else {
        
        digitalWrite(LEDG, HIGH);
        
        digitalWrite(LEDR, LOW);
        
    }
  \end{lstlisting}    
    
    TF\_LITE\_REPORT\_ERROR(error\_reporter, "Person score: \%d No person score: \%d", person\_score, no\_person\_score);
    
    And at a high level, that is how the person\_detection example works! We simply take and crop the input images, pass them to the neural network, and then report the higher scoring result!
    
    Changes for the OV7670
    To use the OV7670 instead of the OV7675, simply make the change you made in the camera test! Change the fourth argument of the call to Camera.begin() as highlighted below, from OV7675 to OV7670. That’s it! The library will handle the rest! You can find the camera initialization in:
    
  \begin{lstlisting}
    // Initialize the OV7675 camera
    void if(!Camera.begin(QCIF, GRAYSCALE, 5, OV7675)) {
        Serial.println("Failed to initialize camera");
        While (1);
    }    
  \end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Understanding the Code in the Multi-Tenant Example}

Now that you have gotten the multi\_tenent application deployed to your microcontroller, let's explore the code a little bit. The main multi\_tenant.ino file is similar to the previous person detection example. The difference here is that we use the shared allocator from TFLite Micro to allocate space for two models using the same tensor arena.

Unlike previous examples where we directly passed the tensor\_arena and kTensorArenaSize to the interpreter, we need to directly create an allocator and pass it to our two separate interpreters.

In the setup() function, we create our allocator that will be shared between both of our interpreters as follows:

\begin{lstlisting}
tflite::MicroAllocator* allocator =

tflite::MicroAllocator::Create(tensor_arena, kTensorArenaSize, error_reporter);
\end{lstlisting}


Then we create the interpreter for the person detection model, passing the allocator to it as a parameter, and then calling AllocateTensors().

\begin{lstlisting}
static tflite::MicroInterpreter vww_static_interpreter(

vww_model, micro_op_resolver, allocator, error_reporter);

vww_interpreter = &vww_static_interpreter;
\end{lstlisting}


\begin{lstlisting}
// allocate the VWW model from tensor_arena

TfLiteStatus allocate_status = vww_interpreter->AllocateTensors();
\end{lstlisting}

Now that both interpreters are created using the same allocator, we can use them as we would normally.

In the loop() function, we first run the micro\_speech model as we did in the KWS example, except this time if the model detects ‘yes’ we capture an image and run the person\_detection model. The LED control is performed in arduino\_detection\_responder.cpp and follows the patterns used in both the micro\_speech and person\_detection examples.

\begin{lstlisting}
bool heardYes = RespondToKWS(error_reporter, found_command,
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}
is_new_command, score);
if(heardYes){
    //Our keyword spotting model heard 'yes' so we detect if a person is visible
    // Get image from provider.
    if (kTfLiteOk != GetImage(error_reporter, kNumCols, kNumRows, kNumChannels,
    vww_input->data.int8)) {
        TF_LITE_REPORT_ERROR(error_reporter, "Image capture failed.");
    }
    
    // Run the model on this input and make sure it succeeds
    if (kTfLiteOk != vww_interpreter->Invoke()) {
        TF_LITE_REPORT_ERROR(error_reporter, "Invoke failed.");
    }
    
    // Process the inference results.
    int8_t person_score = vww_output->data.uint8[kPersonIndex];
    int8_t no_person_score = vww_output->data.uint8[kNotAPersonIndex];
    RespondToDetection(error_reporter, person_score, no_person_score);
    
}
\end{lstlisting}


\subsection{Changes for the OV7670}

To use the OV7670 instead of the OV7675, simply make the change you made in the camera test! Change the fourth argument of the call to Camera.begin() as highlighted below, from OV7675 to OV7670. That’s it! The library will handle the rest! You can find the camera initialization in:

\begin{lstlisting}
// Initialize the OV7675 camera
void if(!Camera.begin(QCIF, GRAYSCALE, 5, OV7675)) {
    Serial.println("Failed to initialize camera");
    While (1);
}
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Recap: Time Series for Anomaly Detection}

\subsection{Recap: Time Series}

In this reading, we will do a quick recap of the time series from Course 2 as we will be leveraging that knowledge for a slightly different application than what we discussed previously (i.e., anomaly detection): gesture recognition with a custom-built magic wand.

\subsection{Supervised vs. Unsupervised Learning}

So far, we have tackled many interesting machine learning tasks that cover a variety of sensory modalities (e.g., vision, audition). All of these tasks required a labeled dataset - a known output value for a given set of inputs - and were thus comfortably situated within the supervised learning paradigm, which we first discussed in Course 1.

However, frequently, we encounter datasets in which our output variable is unknown. Whether because we do not have access to the output variable, or we are merely interested in the internal structure of our dataset, we cannot resort to our standard machine learning framework, which relies on training and test sets. Instead, we must adopt an alternative, equally useful, a paradigm known as unsupervised learning to approach these tasks, which we discussed in Course 2. 

There are still similarities between the supervised and unsupervised learning paradigms. Firstly, both assume the existence of a dataset that is used to train our machine learning algorithm. The performance of both generally improves as more data becomes available. Nevertheless, unsupervised learning is distinct in that we do not require the existence of a predictor variable. Unsupervised learning looks at the internal structure of a dataset and attempts to either group this data systematically, known as clustering, or look for abnormalities within the dataset, known as outlier analysis, or anomaly detection.

\subsection{Anomaly Detection}

In course 2, we were mostly interested in anomaly detection, predominantly due to its potential for predictive maintenance (i.e., noticing abnormalities in machines that can be resolved before catastrophic events). Anomaly detection can be performed spatially or temporally, either looking at associations between different physical locations, or the same location at different points in time.

Most sensory devices are fixed in discrete locations, such as those monitoring the temperature and vibrational characteristics of a piece of machinery. Inherently, these devices output a temporal signal that can be monitored and used to look for anomalies over time. These anomalies manifest in myriad ways: a sudden temperature change, a sudden increase in vibration or sound, or a sudden drop in pressure. Given sufficient explanatory variables, anomaly detection algorithms allow us to detect outliers and perform corrective actions in a timely manner to mitigate the possibility of catastrophic events, such as an error, a machine breakdown, or even an explosion.

Many algorithms exist for the detection of anomalies in time series data. One algorithm that was discussed in the previous course was k-means, which looks at the distance of a data point in feature space from a set of discrete clusters that represent the majority of data points. We saw that one drawback of k-means was its poor performance on high-dimensional data due to the curse of dimensionality. However, we were able to improve performance by reducing the dimensionality of the data with t-SNE. Anomaly detection can also be achieved using neural network structures known as autoencoders, which attempt to collapse knowledge of the dataset in a small set of neurons located at the mid-layer of the autoencoder (this formulation is very similar to principal component analysis). Autoencoders tend to have superior performance to traditional methods due to their ability to capture complex non-linear associations.

\subsection{Magic Wand}

In the remainder of this section, we will leverage this prior knowledge for a slightly different purpose: building in gesture recognition using a custom-built magic wand.

\subsection{TinyML Sensor Ecosystem}

    0:00 / 0:00Klicken Sie auf die Pfeiltaste nach oben, um das Geschwindigkeitsmenu aufzurufen. Regeln Sie dann mit den Hoch und Runter Pfeiltasten die Geschwindigkeit. Klicken Sie Enter, um die jeweilige Geschwindigkeit festzulegen.Geschwindigkeit1.0xKlicke auf diesen Knopf und diess Video stumm oder nicht stumm zu schalten, oder drücke die HOCH oder RUNTER Knöpfe um die Lautstärke zu erhöhen oder abzusenken.Maximal Lautstärke.Videotranskript
    Beginn des Transkriptes, zum Ende springen.
    VIJAY JANAPA REDDI: Hi, in this module, I'm
    very excited to be embarking on a whole new application journey with you which
    is around the magic wand.
    Now let me take this opportunity to talk about the kind of sensors
    that we have been using and what the prospects are
    if we expand the landscape of sensors that we look at specifically for TinyML
    applications.
    Now generally when you talk about machine
    learning as it's applied to sensors, it often
    ends up being focused on two domains.
    It happens to be speech, and it happens to be vision.
    However, when you're talking about embedded sensors,
    there's a large number of sensors out there
    from being able to measure temperature to pressure to humidity.
    To being able to tell the strength of the magnetic field, and so forth.
    There are many different kinds of sensors out there.
    And so if we can tap into some of these rich array of sensors,
    then we might be able to uncover a whole new range of applications that are
    specifically unique to TinyML itself.
    And not just the usual machine learning applications
    from just speech and vision.
    Now we will specifically be talking about the IMU.
    The inertial measurement unit is a really rich sensor.
    It generates a lot of data in a very short amount of time.
    And it gives you very rich information as well
    if you know how to deal with the data that's coming through it.
    Specifically, a 9 degree of freedom IMU typically has three sensors in it.
    It's got the accelerometer, the gyroscope, and the magnetometer.
    You can do really interesting things by collecting
    the data that's coming from the sensors, and fusing them together.
    For example, the three axis gyroscope has the ability
    to give us rotation data around a known point.
    While the accelerometer has the ability to tell us about
    displacements in the x-axis, y-axis, and z-axis.
    Now a post processing step down on the microcontroller
    might actually allow us to infer some interesting properties
    about the experience of the sensor as a whole.
    And do so very robustly because each of these sensors
    has some pitfalls that I'll talk about later.
    Now why am I going on and on about the IMU?
    Well, I'm trying to get you excited about the possibilities of using
    other kinds of sensors.
    Now a real practical reason as to why you would consider other sensors out
    of things like microphones and just cameras
    is really because of power consumption.
    Now remember TinyML is all about running on ultra low power consumption devices
    as much as using just a small coin cell battery.
    Well, let's look at a sensor here.
    If you look at the image sensor, it consumes around 98 milliwatts.
    But if you look at a standard IMU sensor,
    it only consumes about 9.5 milliwatts.
    That's an order of magnitude difference in the power consumption.
    So if you think about the TinyML system or the device,
    then the sensor that you use can actually make a very big difference.
    Now, of course, this depends on the application
    that you're choosing to build.
    But the general point I'm getting across here
    is that these very small little sensors have the ability
    to consume very low power.
    And still they can enable very rich application experiences.
    Moreover, you also want to think about the size of the device
    because you want these sensors to be really small so you can put them
    everywhere.
    And they have got to be lightweight, especially for robotic applications.
    And you want them to be really cheap so you can use a lot of them
    and do very interesting TinyML applications using them.
    Because of their low cost and because of the low power consumption,
    there are countless applications.
    For example, you can do embedded sensing on bodies for activities like sports
    or activity recognition or detecting medical [INAUDIBLE] before they
    actually become problematic.
    You can enable a rich number of robotic applications.
    Specifically IMUs are very useful for things like stabilization.
    And, of course, IMUs are already used today in things like the Apple Watch
    for instance, which actually detects a person suddenly falling down.
    And then can actually alert an emergency health worker.
    Now there are many different applications.
    Now you can think of many other ones well beyond the ones
    that I'm actually showing you here.
    All that sounds exciting.
    But what are some challenges?
    Well, there are some gotchas.
    And they're largely about interpretability, sensor drift,
    and deployment sensitivity.
    Interpretability is the notion that when you look at the sensor data,
    can you really make sense of it?
    For example, we can see images.
    For example, we can hear the audio that we're actually playing.
    But when you look at some of the sensor data that's
    coming in from things like the IMU, we don't really
    have an easy way to interpret that kind of data.
    And that kind of makes it a little challenging
    to be able to easily build applications.
    That's the first thing.
    The second thing is that the sensors are not quite ideal.
    In other words, sensors tend to behave differently over time.
    Now an ideal sensor would be one that has
    the exact same behavior on day 1,000 as it does for instance, on day zero.
    Well turns out, that many of these small little sensors
    tend to have problems like drift for example.
    Gyroscopes are specifically subject to bias instabilities.
    Now, that means that over time their initial zero reading ends up drifting.
    And that can be problematic if you have made some sort of assumptions about how
    to engineer the application based on day zero
    and then deployed it into the field.
    But 1,000 days afterward, the sensor has a certain drift.
    That means you've got to suddenly compensate for that.
    But how would you do it if you already deployed into the field?
    Now, accelerometers commercially, they're sensitive to vibration.
    That any kind of non gravity accelerations can also affect them.
    So you have sensors that have this capability,
    but they also come with the pitfalls.
    And so you want to understand some of this in order
    to be able to use them effectively in applications.
    Last one is really around deployment sensitivity.
    You often have to calibrate or fine tune some of these sensors.
    There tend to be these minor differences that
    can actually have a major effect on the application outcome.
    Some of which we'll actually see as we build a magic wand application.
    These differences often end up manifesting
    in terms of biases in the data that you have to compensate
    for in the pre-processing stage.
    And we'll look at that in our application.
    All that said, the thing that I'm trying to get you excited about
    is the fact that there are countless applications that can use one or more
    of these sensor for purposes that are well beyond the scope of what
    we are covering here.
    We will specifically focus on the magic wand application
    that uses the accelerometer and the gyroscope inside the IMU.
    But keep in mind that that's just the tip of the iceberg,
    and I'm looking forward to seeing some of the projects you will all build
    after you're done with this course.
    That said, let's get started with the magic wand application.

\section{Anatomy of an IMU}

\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-8.8.png} 

Icons for Accelerometer, Magnetometer, and Gyroscope from the previous video. The acceleraometer shows a square with arrows in three directions indicating the XYZ planes. The magnetometer shows a standard U-shaped magnet. The gyroscope shows a 3D gyroscope, indicating rotations in 3 dimmensions.
    
An inertial measurement unit (IMU) is a system composed of sensors that relay information about a device’s movement, such as accelerometers, gyroscopes, and magnetometers. In concert, each of the sensors provides a detailed account of a device’s instantaneous motion, orientation, and acceleration, which are often used for navigational and stabilizing purposes in vehicles and aircraft as part of electronic feedback control systems. We will be using it for our Magic Wand application. The Arduino Nano 33 BLE sense has a 9-axis IMU.
    
    More recently, applications of the IMU to wearable devices have been stimulated by the quantified self-movement. The data generated from IMU’s can be used for machine learning purposes in classifying different types of actions associated with a device. For example, by attaching an IMU to a specific body part, such as an arm, it may be possible to discern different gestures purely from time-series information, such as waving, shaking hands, or typing on a keyboard. This makes the IMU particularly useful for embedded machine learning applications that are gesture-activated, such as lighting up your phone screen when picking it up, when checking the time on a smartwatch, or even fall detection.
    
\subsection{Structure of an IMU}

    An IMU consists of at least two of the following sensors: an accelerometer, a gyroscope, and a magnetometer. In principle, these devices can be designed to provide single- or multi-axis measurements, but most offer multi-axis measurements to provide an accurate set of information associated with the position and orientation of a device in three-dimensional space. The importance of the IMU for sensing applications is highlighted by our Arduino Nano BLE Sense, which comes packaged with a built-in 9-axis IMU.
 
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{TinyML/3 Development/4-8.9.png} 
    
    Image of the Arduino with arrows for acceleration and rotation drawn on it. We see three acceleration and three rotation arrows showing that we are measuring XYZ acceleration and roll, pitch, yaw rotations.
    
    The three sensors that constitute an IMU afford for the following capabilities:
    
    \textbf{Accelerometer.} Measures changes in velocity (acceleration) and position (velocity), as well as absolute orientation. The accelerometer is the device in tablets and smartphones which ensures the image on-screen remains upright regardless of orientation. By itself, the accelerometer provides information about the linear and rotational X-, Y-, and Z- directions. The accelerometer allows the 3-axis of motion to be captured. An onboard accelerometer is modeled as a micromechanical damped mass-spring system, wherein the compression or extension of the mass-spring system can be mathematically related to an object’s acceleration.
    
    \textbf{Magnetometer.} Establishes cardinal direction (directional heading). A simple example of a magnetometer is a compass, which is used to measure the direction of the Earth’s magnetic field. Smaller versions called microelectromechanical magnetic field sensors can be incorporated into integrated circuits, allowing them to be combined with other sensors as part of an IMU. The magnetometer measures orientation to magnetic north on the X, Y, and Z axes. Together with the accelerometer, the 6 degrees-of-freedom of a system can be accounted for, which fully describes the kinematics of a system. Oftentimes, magnetometers work via the Hall effect, which involves the creation of a potential difference across a conductor as a result of a perpendicular applied magnetic field.
    
    \textbf{Gyroscope.} Measures changes in orientation (rotation) and rotational velocity. Microelectromechanical gyroscopes, often called gyro meters, are present in many consumer electronics such as gaming controllers. A gyroscope provides information about the rotational X- (roll), Y- (pitch), and Z- (yaw) directions. The 6 degree-of-freedom system created by combining a magnetometer and accelerometer suffers from several shortcomings, such as the sensitivity of magnetometers to time-varying magnetic disturbances, and the corruption of accelerometer readings due to the presence of linear acceleration distorting the Earth gravity vector.
    
    To alleviate these shortcomings, a 3-axis gyroscope can be added, creating a 9 degree-of-freedom, or “gyro-stabilized” system. The gyroscope provides the system with an independent measurement of instantaneous rotation speed, complementing the original 6 degree-of-freedom system. The gyroscope works by the conservation of angular momentum; a spinning wheel or disc rotates at high speed, while the spin axis is freely allowed to rotate and assume any orientation. The inertia of the spinning wheel allows it to remain unperturbed in the same direction during tilting or rotation, allowing rotational information to be discerned.
    
\subsection{Next Steps}
    In the following sections, we will focus on obtaining time-series information from the onboard IMU, and subsequently, use this information to train a machine learning model capable of detecting specific actions of a magic wand.
